SUMIIARY AND COINCLUSIONS
i.        The proposed project, the Bank's first agricultural credit project
covering all of Yugoslavia, aims to increase agricultural production by ex-
tending credit to social and private sector farms (in all Republics and Auto-
nomous Provinces) and to improve resource allocation by supporting institution-
building within the Bank of Vojvodina (VB) and the seven participating banks
(PBs).
ii.       Although agriculture's role has been declining relatively with rapid
industrialization, it still remains an important sector in Yugoslavia's econo-
mic development. Almost 40% of the population still lives in rural areas.
Agriculture accounts for 20% of the social product, constitutes roughly 15% -
20% of total commodity exports, with livestock, meat, fruits and vegetables
accounting for about two-thirds of agricultural exports, and employs about
44% of the labor force. The social product in agriculture increased at an
average rate of 3.2% per year during 1968-73, with the social sector output
increasing annuially at 5.2% and the private sector at 2.4%. Agricultural
production fluctuates heavily with weather conditions and its growth has not
kept pace with consumption growth. The major constraints to further agricul-
tural development in the social sector are the lack of capital for large in-
vestments needed in crop and livestock production and processing facilities.
The private sector's main constraints are lack of capital and credit facili-
ties for on-farm mechanization and purchase of better quality breeds and fer-
tilizers, and inadequate extension services for farmers not participating in
cooperative arrangements with the social sector. The Agricultural Development
Plan in recognizing this gives priority to large investments in the problem
areas and emphasizes cooperation between the social and private sectors. The
proposed nroject is part of the Plan's development program. It is in line
with the Bank's lending strategy for the agricultural sector in aiming at
production growth, and support for private farmers.
iii.      The project would increase production of maize, fodder crops, milk,
meat, vegetables and fruits, by about Din 1,140 million (US$67 million) per
aiauum, and expand agricultural processing capacity. It would support the
Government's objective of self-sufficiency in agricultural products and in-
crease of export production. The proposed loan would provide funds (a) to
about 380 social sector enterprises for investments in dairy farms, cattle
fattening, vegetable production, irrigation, drainage, land development, cold
storage, grain drying and milk collection facilities; (b) to about 10,200
private farmers for investments in dairy, sheep, vegetable and fruit produc-
tion, on-farm mechanization and land development.
iv.       The project would cost Din 2,125 million (US$125 million) of which
20% or Din 430 million (US$25 million) would be foreign exchange. The Bank
loan would finance US$50 million or 40% of total project cost. VB would be
the Borrower and serve as the apex institution in administering project im-
plementation.
v.        VB would channel the loan proceeds to a PB in each Republic and
Province, at 9.5% interest with a repayment period of 15 years including 4
years grace except in Vojvodina where VB would also implement the investment



- ii -
program. Social sector and private sector subborrowers would benefit from
loans up to 14 years paying 11% on the proceeds of the Bank loan and the
prevailing rates on the local funds.
vi.       Procurement would be done by the subborrowers under the supervision
of the VB and the PBs. The cold storage facilities and planting and harvest-
ing equipment for the social sector subprojects would be procured under inter-
national competitive bidding according to the Bank's guidelines. Due to
limited availability domestically, about 1,400 in-calf heifers for the social
sector dairy farm subprojects would be procured from abroad through solicita-
tion of bids from suppliers in at least three countries. All other items
would be procured through existing commercial channels or in accordance with
local bidding procedures satisfactory to the Bank.
vii.      The project would contribute to the production targets outlined in
the Agricultural Development Plan. -It would contribute to achieving self-
sufficiency in feed grains, milk and milk products, and increase exports of
meat, fruits and vegetables. While the project's incremental production would
largely be marketed domestically, total net foreign exchange earnings or savings
over the project's 20 year lifetime would amount to about US$200 million. The
project would generate about 6,000 new permanent employment opportunities and
reduce underemployment for about 10,000 farmers. In addition, about 8,700 jobs
would be created during the 3 year investment period. Almost 40% of the pro-
posed loan would directly or indirectly benefit an estimated 39,000 poorer
farmers. The overall economic rate of return of the project investments in-
cluding administrative and training expenses would be 27%, exclusive of pos-
sible benefits from improved resource allocation.
viii.     Agreement having been reached on the principal issues the project
would be suitable for a Bank loan of US$50 million, for a term of 15 years,
including a grace period of 4 years.



APPRAISAL OF
AN AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
I.  INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of the Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia has
requested a Bank loan for an agricultural credit project to increase agricul-
tural production through investments in livestock, fruit and vegetable growing,
on-farm mechanization, land reclamation and agricultural processing facilities.
The project would help finance subprojects in both the social and private sec-
tors in all Republics and Provinces. The proposed project aims to increase
the quantities of agricultural products primarily for supplying domestic de-
mand, to improve through credit the productivity and income of the rural popu-
lation, particularly private farmers and to strengthen the appraisal capacity
of the participating banks. The proposed project is in line with the Inter-
Republican Agreement for the Long Term Development of Agriculture (Agricultural
Development Plan). The investments to be financed under the project are in-
cluded in the priority list established and agreed upon in the Agricultural
Development Plan (Annex 2).
1.02      Although three previous IBRD loans included a small agricultural
component, the first integrated agricultural development project the Bank
assisted in was the 1973 Agricultural Industries Project in Macedonia (894-YU).
The proposed agricultural credit project would be the first one that the Bank
would participate in for the whole country. The idea of a project in the
agricultural sector came out of the work of IBRD economic missions in 1970
and 1971 and evolved through a continuing dialogue between the Yugoslav gov-
ernment and the Bank on the scope, content and structure of the project.
Preparation was done jointly by the Federal Committee for Agriculture and
the Bank of Vojvodina (VB), assisted by IBRD preparation missions in January
and July 1974.
1.03      This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission in
September/October 1974, consisting of Messrs. Schertz, Heidhues, Lenot (IBRD),
Fagerberg, Percival and Weinschenck (Consultants).
II. THE AGRICULTURAL SECTOR
Geography and Climate
2.01      Of Yugoslavia's total area, about 58% is recorded as agricultural
land of which 70% is arable. The extremely fertile soils predominantly located
in Vojvodina and the northern regions of Croatia, represent only one-sixth of
the country's total area but contain nearly a third of the arable land and
produce more than half of the country's output of wheat and maize and over
three-quarters of its sugar beet. These regions are also suitable for inten-
sive livestock and vegetable production. A mixed farm pattern prevails in



- 2 -
the hills (land up to 500 meters in altitude) accounting for about 407 of
the total area and covering much of Serbia, Slovenia and Bosnia. Important
lines of production are livestock raising and fruit growing and, to a lesser
degree, farm crops. The mountain region covering 44% of the country is gene-
rally unfavorable for crop production, but extensive upland pastures make
possible sheep and some cattle raising. Along the Adriatic coast, vineyards
and some olive and citrus groves are found whereas in southern Macedonia,
Mediterranean crops, such as tobacco, cotton and rice are grown.
2.02      There are three basic climatic zones in Yugoslavia:  (i) the
Mediterranean, covering the Adriatic coastline and southern Macedonia, (ii)
the modified Atlantic, prevalent in Slovenia, Croatia and parts of Serbia,
and (iii) the Continental climatic zones most characteristic in Vojvodina.
Due to the topographic configuration of the courtry these climatic zones are
much modified by the terrain.
2.03      Agricultural production fluctuates heavily with varying weather con-
ditions. Irrigation and drainage are at a low level. Modern irrigation covers
only about 100,000 ha or 1% of the arable land as compared with an irrigation
potential of 3.1 million ha. The need for drainage is most pronounced in
Croatia, Slovenia and Montenegro where non-permeable subsoils prevent the full
development of the crop production potential. The area requiring drainage in
these Republics comprises 1.2 million ha.
Agriculture in the Economy
2.04      In Yugoslavia almost 40% of the population still lives in rural areas.
Agriculture accounts for 20% of the social product 1/, constitutes roughly
15-20% of total commodity exports, with livestock, meat, fruits and vegetables
accounting for about two-thirds of agricultural exports, and employs about 44%
of the labor force of Yugoslavia. The social product from agriculture in-
creased at an average rate of 3.2 percent per year during 1968-73, with the
social sector output increasing at about 5.2% per year and the private sector
at about 2.4%. Average per capita income in agriculture amounts to 42% of
that in the rest of the economy.
The Social and Private Sectors in Agriculture
2.05      One of the important characteristic features of Yugoslav agriculture
is the separate existence of two sectors viz, social and private with different
farm structures. The social sector includes (i) agro-industrial kombinats
(integrated factory farms); (ii) agricultural kombinats (both general agricul-
ture and livestock farms); and (iii) general agricultural cooperatives with
their own agricultural production as well as provision of services to indivi-
dual farmers; these organizations operate under the principles of social owner-
ship of the means of production and workers' self-management. During recent
1/   The social product concept differs from the GNP concept, as the former
excludes certain services, such as public administration, defense,
education, health and social insurance.



- 3 -
years many kombinats have established cooperative units either by assimilation
of existing cooperatives or by formation of new units, in order to link part
of the production of individual farms to their processing industry. The social
sector in 1973 held 15% of the cultivated land, employed 6% of total agricul-
tural manpower, produced 33% of the agricultural product and 45% of the market
production. In 1973 there were 1,745 social sector holdings averaging 1,247
hectares of cultivated area per holding.
2.06      The social sector absorbed more than two-thirds of the gross fixed
investment in agriculture in the post war period. Investments were character-
ized by high and increasing capital intensity with the fixed capital per worker
increasing from 27 thousand dinars (1966 prices) in 1961 to over 96 thousand
dinars in 1970. At the same time the capital output ratio has increased from
an average of 2.86:1 during 1957-61 to 4.43:1 during 1968-70.
2.07      Tlhe pjivate sector comprises about 2.6 million small peasant holdings
of an average size of 3.9 ha.  It has 96% of the total agricultural population
and 85% of cultivated land. The size of private holdings is limited to the
equivalent of 10 ha of arable land. Productivity in the private sector is
significantly lower than in the social sector due to inferior land, seeds and
livestock breeds, lower fertilizer consumption, limited mechanization and lack
of extension :ervice.
2.08      The private and social sectors are for the most part complementary
rather than competitive. Labor-intensive crops, such as vegetables and tobacco,
are almost exclusively produced in the private sector, which also has 95% of
the cattle and 90% of the sheep. Cooperation between the social and private
sectors takes the form of trade in inputs and output, provision of services
by the social sector such as supply of fertilizers, harvesting, cultivations,
marketing and credit, and the contracting of joint production. The social
sector also provides most of the extension service available in Yugoslavia.
About 30% of all smallholders cooperate with the social sector in one way or
-nother, particularly in the more developed and fertile parts of Yugoslavia,
frequently because the food processing facilities in the social sector enter-
prises need inputs from neighboring private farmers. The very small farmers,
particularly in the underdeveloped mountainous regions, are least affected by
sucl- cooperation due to the small number and the specialization of social
sector enterprises.
Marketing (Annex 1, paras 22-25)
2.09      About half of the agricultural production is marlZeted through the
social sector enterprises; the rest is either consumed by the farmers or sold
on local markets. Some of the kombinats have their own arrangements for export
marketing, particularly in the case of processed fruits, vegetables and wine.
This has created numerous problems in export marketing, such as a lack of
quality and standardization control. In order to centralize and coordinate
export marketing, in particular of fruit and vegetables, the Federal Govern-
ment is establishing export agencies in major European markets.



2.10      Due to their smaller scale of production, private farmers rely on
the processing capabilities of kombinats and cooperatives for their industrial
crops. As they also lack marketing information and facilities, private farm-
ers market their produce primarily through cooperatives and kombinats, on
terms and under conditions generally satisfactory to them. The remainder of
their production, mainly vegetables, is sold in local markets where prices
may be higher than those paid by cooperatives and kombinats.
Research and Training
2.11      Research, education and provision of technical services are provided
by some 50 independent institutes for research, dealing with specialized agri-
cultural problems. The institutes are financed by the republics and the com-
iuiunes, and by payments for contract work done for enterprises. The univer-
sities are the training ground for managerial staff, and other technical
workers.  In addition there are some 200 educational centers where farm
workers are trained.
Taxation
2.12      Social sector agricultural enterprises pay income and turnover taxes
and various compulsory and statutory contributions, in much the same way as
industrial enterprises. However, agricultural investments and most inputs
are exempted from turnover taxation. The income tax of kombinats and coop-
eratives is in most Republics and Provinces proportional to their income. 1/
The tax rate varies by region ranging from 0.8% to 7.5%. Compulsory and sta-
tutory contributions include a surcharge for social insurance, allocations to
the collective consumption fund, 2/ business and reserve funds and payments
to the Skopje Earthquake Reconstruction Fund and the Fund for the Accelerated
Development of the Less Developed Regions. Private farmers pay a tax accord-
ing to the cadastral value of their agricultural land. The valuation is based
on the estimated productivity of the land, with each Republic and Province ap-
plying its own rules in establishing yields, production costs and prices to
be used in arriving at cadastral income. The valuation is updated in five
year intervals. In some Republics private farmers pay a special tax on in-
come from off-farm services; the tax rate is established by the commune.
Development Constraints
2.13      The main constraint to further development of the social sector
agriculture is the lack of capital for investments in crop and livestock pro-
duction and processing facilities. The social sector with its modern produc-
tion techniques and relatively high yields given the land resources available
and the climatic conditions, requires large investments in irrigation, drainage
1/   Only Serbia and Kosovo have instituted a progressive income tax system
with tax rates ranging between 0.5% and 13%.
2/   The collective consumption fund finances schools, scholarships and various
social facilities, such as vacation houses.



- 5 -
and land development to further increase productivity. Expanded grain and
fodder production would allow increased livestock production without unduly
raising imports of feed grains which are being restricted due to Yugoslavia's
shortage of foreign exchange.
2.14      The main contraints for further developrment of agriculture in the
private sector are lack of capital and credit facilities for the purchase of
better quality breeds, seeds, fertilizers and tractors and machinery, and
inadequate extension services for farmers not participating in cooperative
arrangements with the social sector.
DevelopmntStrategy and Priorities
2.15      After lengthy discussions, the Republics and Autonomous Provinces, in
July 1973, agreed on a common agricultural policy for 1973-1985, the "Inter-
republican Agreement on the Long-Term Development Policy for Agriculture" (the
Agricultural Development Plan). 1/ The Agricultural Development Plan's 2/
basic objective is to raise produ;ction to meet expected domestic needs and
allow for some exports. It emphasizes (a) the further development of the
social sector through modernization of production techniques, expansion of
processing facilities, better utilization of available resources and the ad-
justment of production to changes in demand; and (b) the development of pri-
vate farms primarily through cooperation with the social sector to ensure
further progress in the socialization of agriculture and rural areas. The
Plan's long-run growth objectives were revised in 1974 and are now defined
in the "Basic Principles of the Common Policy for Long-Term Development of
Yugoslavia until 1985" as 3.5% per annum agricultural production growth for
the period ending 1985, with the social sector growing at 7.5% and the private
sector at 2%. Emphasis is given to livestock production, which is projected
to increase at 5% per year as compared with 3% for plant production. Annual
investments during 1976-1985 are planned at about 6.3 billion dinars at 1974
prices, more than half of them in the private sector. Investment financing
would be provided by enterprises' and farmers' own contributions (40%), com-
mercial banks and state and federal funds (40%) and bilateral foreign credits,
IBRD credits and special subsidies (20%) (Annex 2, paras 15 - 18). Planned
increases in meat, fruits and early vegetables are geared to sales expecta-
tions abroad, particularly in the EEC countries.
2.16      To achieve its objectives the Plan focuses on price policies, in-
vestment policies, and cooperation between the social and private sectors.
Minimum prices for cereals will be established on the basis of average produc-
tion costs on social sector holdings, periodically adjusted along with rising
production costs. Minimum prices for livestock will be fixed in relation to
the corn price (Annex 2, para. 14). Special incentives are foreseen to
1/   The Agreement is frequently referred to as the "Green Plan" of Yugos-
lavia.
2/   The Plan is discussed in detail in Annex 2.



- 6 -
encourage mobilization of enterprises' own resources, such as income tax
rebates for agricultural investments, turnover tax exemptions and interest
rate subsidies for investment credits, reduction of import duties on imports
of agricultural machinery that is not produced in Yugoslavia, elimination of
the dinar deposit requirement 1/ with the National Bank for foreign credits
financing agricultural investments, and an increase in the foreign exchange
retention quota from depreciation charges. 2/  Expanded cooperation amo.zg pri-
vate farmers and between private farmers and social sector organizations would
be supported through provision of financing, and interest rate and tax poli-
cies. Cooperating private farmers may also join the health, social insurance
and pension schemes of social sector organizations.
2.17      The Agricultural Development Plan, for the first time in Yugoslavia's
postwar development, provides a comprehensive guideline for the development
of agriculture, encompassing both the social and the private sector. In re-
cognizing the potential of the private sector and explicitly supporting its
development, the Plan is a big step forward towards better resource utiliza-
tion and more even income distribution, although the impact of the multipli-
city of interest rates on the achievement of these objectives should be studied
carefully. However, in supporting almost exclusively those private farmers
who either forn a cooperative or cooperate in some way with the social sector,
the Plan will not reach a sizeable portion of the agricultural population,
notably the smaller farmers and those in the mountainous and infertile areas.
2.18      The Plan's projected growth rate for the social sector seems to be
too optimistic. The Plan apparently gives less than due weight to the fact
that the declining growth rates in the social sector since 1965/66 indicate
a change in the growth pattern; for the social sector with the land and tech-
nology available at present and with its high level of yields the average
annual production increase over a period of 12 years is unlikely to exceed 3%.
Higher growth rates seem to be possible in production lines with no or little
input of land, such as livestock. However, their production increase above
3% - 4% per year would require increasing imports of corn, which the Plan does
not foresee. The planned growth rates for the private sector seem achievable.
If realized, they would, however, imply a growing income discrepancy between
the private and social sectors since the labor force in the private sector is
unlikely to decrease much. The Plan's objectives of achieving better resource
utilization and increased productivity in the private sector, and enhanced
contract cooperation, which is concentrated on the larger farms for reasons
of economies of scale, would be supported by continued migration and resulting
increases in farm size which, however, are limited by the present regulations
on farm size.
1/   Enterprises and banks borrowing abroad are required to deposit in dinars
a certain percentage of the foreign credit with the National Bank without
earning interest.
2/   Enterprises are entitled to retain in foreign exchange 10% of the annual
depreciation charges.

III. AGRICULTURAL CREDIT
A. General
3.01      Total Yugoslav agricultural investments in fixed assets in 1972
amounted to Din 3.1 billion ($182 million) of which an average of 34% was
financed by investors' own funds; banks provided an average of 41% and public
funds provided more than 50% in the less developed and about 13% in the more
developed Republics and Provinces.
3.02      Most Yugoslav enterprises have debt/equity ratios far higher than
those found in most capitalist countries. The "self management" system has
accentuated the problem of low retention of income as reserves, since most of
the income is distributed to the workers. Enterprises are heavily dependent
on credit and are not always able to meet their loan repayment obligations.
The banks have had to support enterprises in difficulty by rescheduling loans,
providing new loans and strengthening the enterprises' management.
3.03      Yugoslavia's interest rate policy should be seen in the political
and economic context of the country's socialist self-management system. Under
the present decentralized system, factor prices are beginning to play an
increasing role in resource allocation, although the trend toward higher and
more realistic interest rates is delayed by non-economic factors. The banking
structure wlhere the borrowers are at the same time the bank's major share-
holders (founders), has contributed to relatively low interest rates. Further-
nore, federal, state and communal funds are provided at low cost or used to
subsidize interest rates. The Yugoslavs consider low interest rates on in-
vestment credits an important policy instrument for development purposes,
especially in the less developed regions and in the agricultural sector. Also,
provisions for risk are low as they reflect the low occurrence of defaults
resulting from the particular relationship between banks and enterprises.
3.04      The interest rate structure is as follows:
Deposits (depending upon notice) 4 to 10%
Short-Term Loans                  11 to 12%
"Commercial" Long-Term Loans    10 to 12%
Investment Loans from the
Federal Fund for Development    4 to 6%
Investment Loans to Agriculture:
Social Sector                3 to 12%
Private Sector               3 to 6%



-8-
Rates on loans and deposits are fixed each year by the Assembly of Founders
of each bank and are substantially in the same range across the country.
3.05      The present interest rates imply low or negative real rates which
contribute to a permanent excess demand for investable resources and high
capital intensity, thus aggravating the unemployment or underemployment
problem, especially in agriculture and in the less developed regions. With
the price of capital low, its rationing is often done on non-economic grounds
especially at the inter-regional or inter-sectoral level, and often for reasons
that could not possibly be met by an allocation based on the price of capital
alone. At the enterprise level, however, interest rates seem to play a larger
role in resource allocation, particularly in influencing the choice of produc-
tion techniques. Finally, one result of the excess demand for capital has
been the above-mentioned inability of enterprises to meet financial obligations
on schedule. 1/ A study of interest rates in the agricultural sector will be
undertaken as part of the Project (paza. 5.17 and Annex 7G).
3.06      in general credit is provided to a private farmer through a social
sector organization with which he has a cooperation agreement (Annex 7, A).
This mechanism appears to work satisfactorily. Private farmers may also ob-
tain credit directly from the banks, usually on less favorable terms.
B. The Banking System
3.07      The Basic Economic Report 2/ explains in detail the development of
the Yugoslav Banking System since 1947, and the legal, financial and operation-
al framework within which banks operate is described in Annex 7B. Banks are
owned and controlled by the enterprises (founders) of the region where they
operate, who are also borrowers from the banks. The organization, ownership,
operations, functions and powers of the banks' management organs and branches
are governed by the statutes and the self-management agreements of each bank.
In addition to its statutes, each bank operates according to an annual
"Business Policy" which implies preparing, against the background of the Five-
Year Plan, a program statement about the Bank's main objectives, magnitude of
operations and interest rates structure. The system appears in general to work
satisfactorily, providing efficient coordination between the investment plans
of enterprises and availability of resources according to priorities as out-
lined by the Plan. The banks have to provide finance for investments according
to the Plan and in particular for agricultural investments according to the
priorities established by the Green Plan.
1/   See IBRD, The Economic Development of Yugoslavia, Report No. 194a-YU,
November 26, 1973, pp. 285-287.
2/   See IBRD, The Economic Development of Yugoslavia, Report No. 194a-YU,
November 26, 1973, pp. 242-275.



- 9 -
C.  The Bank of Vojvodina (Annex 7,
3.08      The Bank of Vojvodina (VB) has been agreed upon in the Agricultural
Development Plan as the borrower and apex 1/ bank for the project. An important
part of its current operations deals with the agricultural sector. VB has a
Head Office and 14 branches covering most of the Province of Vojvodina and two
offices outside Vojvodina, in Belgrade and Zagreb. VB, headed by a General
Manager and supported by a qualified senior staff, has about 1,000 staff. VB
is more fully described in Annex 7, C. VB staff involved in appraisal of in-
vestment loans appear capable, but lack experience in applying financial and
economic criteria in project appraisal. However, with adequate training under
the Project, VB's staff would be able to improve their appraisal techniques.
3.09      VB has a Business Policy and other "decisions" made by the Founders'
Assembly to guide its operations each year. A prominent feature is that VB's
or any branch off'ice's exposure to any client should not exceed 30% of its
total outstanding loans (actually no borrower has more than 10% of the total
outstanding portfo]io).
3.10      Investment proposals that enterprises submit to VB for financing are
appraised and approved in a branch office, or in the head office, depending on
the size or complexity of the loans. Several weaknesses are apparent in VB
appraisal reports, particularly insufficient technical appraisal, inadequate
market and economic analysis and, at times, unsatisfactory financial analysis.
3.11      VB operates as a mixed comercial/investment bank and is authorized
to perform foreign exchange transactions. Supervision of investments, with
regard to the financial position of the borrowers and to procurement arrange-
ments is satisfactory. Disbursement methods are sound.
3.12      In 1973, short-term loan commitments were 2.9 billion dinars of
which about one-third went to agriculture (Annex 7, Table 1). Short-term
loan portfolio as a percentage of total assets was about 30%. In 1973, long-
term loan commitments were 1.3 billion dinars of which about 28% went to
agriculture (Annex 7, Table 1). In 1973, VB's guarantees totalled Din 627
million of which Din 284 million were in foreign exchange (Annex 7, Table 2).
3.13      As a general policy the short-term resources are used almost exclu-
sively for short-term investments, although a prudent usage of short-term re-
sources for long-term investments, under the close supervision of the National
Bank, has been allowed since end 1971. Most short-term resources are deposits
and borrowings from the National Bank through rediscount facilities (Annex 7,
Table 5).
3.14      Of long-term loans to agriculture, 83% go to social sector enter-
prises for their own investments, 16% for relending to private farmers, and
less thaxn 1% directly to private farmers. Most of the loans to the social
sector are small (below 5 million dinars) and for a term of 5 to 10 years.
The average size of a long-term loan for agriculture is Din 3.3 million for
the social sector, Din 1.2 million for loans to the social sector relent to
1/   i.e. the primary bank, which will act as intermediary between the
Participating Banks and IBRD.



- 10 -
private farmers and Din 40,000 for loans directly to private farmers. VB
provides guarantees for suppliers' credits and other loans, but does not make
provision from its long-term resources against these contingent liabilities.
Guarantees amount to close to 50% of long-term loans but defaults have been
small (about 2%) and the risk does not appear significant.
3.15      The income statement of VB appears in Annex 7, Table 3.  VB like
any other Yugoslav bank, being owned by its founders-borrowers, does not
consider profitability an essential measure of its efficiency and rinancial
performance. Gross income less direct financial expenses in 1973 was about
1.7% of average total assets, which is low and reflects both the mixed nature
and the non-profit orientation of VB. Administrative expenses in 1973 were
about 0.6% of average total assets, which is also low and shows strict control
over the administrative budget. In 1973, dividends to the founders were 4.5%
of equity. 1/
3.16      The balance sheet of VB is in Annex 7, Table 4.  The current ratio
in 1973 was 0.82:1 which is low. However, VB complies with the National Bank
liquidity requirements (Annex 7B) and its liquidity position is adequate.
The long-term debt/equity ratio was 4.6:1 in 1973; it does not fully reflect
the financial cushion available to creditors, since part of the debt is owed
to the Province and the Federation who are unlikely to force liquidation in
case of financial crisis. Excluding these, the ratio becomes 3.1:1.
3.17      In accordance with Yugoslav accounting practice, financial statements
of banks are not audited but simply certified by the Federal Social Accounting
Services (SAS) (see para. 5.22). Therefore, an accurate evaluation of the
quality of VB's investment portfolio and the adequacy of its provisions is
difficult. At the end of 1973, provisions against potential losses amounted
to 2.2%, write offs to 0.12% (12 million dinars), and arrears to 0.9% of the
investment portfolio; all arrears were for less than one year (Annex 7, Table
6); reschedulings were negligible.
3.18      Along conventional lines, it is not possible, pending an audit, to
establish the credit-worthiness of VB as an independent organization. In the
Yugoslav environment, IBRD's normal criteria of credit-worthiness have a li-
mited meaning. Nevertheless, it is likely that VB will be in a position to
meet its obligations to the Bank. Its founders have unlimited liability
toward VB's obligations, it is closely integrated with the economy of Vojvo-
dina and its liquidation would be inconceivable. Its financial management has
been adequate with no significant losses, arrears have been low, and obliga-
tions have been met satisfactorilly. VB's loan portfolio,appears sound, and
adequate provisions for risk are made.
3.19      In Yugoslavia, investment planning is limited to one -ear.  Longer
term forecasts are, therefore, subject to margins of errors larger than usual.
1/   Including housing and public works fund.



- 11 -
Forecasts are at best rough and based on inadequate information and defective
assumptions. With this proviso, forecasts have been prepared for VB's opera-
tions (Annex 7, Table 7) and for its financial position (Annex 7, Table 9).
VB's financial position is expected to remain satisfactory and profits are
projected to increase to about 7.5% of equity.
D. The Participating Banks -/ (Annex 7,D)
3.20      Only one bank from each Republic and Autonomous Province would par-
ticipate under the project, and VB would function as a participating bank for
Vojvodina. The eight banks, which are among the most important in Yugoslavia,
account together for 70% to 75% of the total banking activity.
3.21      The organization and management bodies, including head and branch
offices, of the PBs are similar to that of VB. However, UBZ, the PB for
Croatia, is a "federation" of five independent banks which are jointly liable
for UBZ operations. In general, management and staff of the PBs in the more
developed regions appear more sophisticated and experienced, and their apprai-
sal procedures seem better. Most of the PBs are already known to the Bank as
borrowers or as disbursement agents.
3.22      The management and the staff of the banks appear generally exper-
ienced and capable. In each of the PBs, the unit responsible for handling
long-term loans to agriculture would be responsible for subloans under the
project; in each of these units there are several agronomists and agricul-
tural engineers in addition to economists, financial analysts and usually
lawyers. These staff seem to be in general competent, but not skilled in the
use of financial and economic criteria for project appraisal but training
exercises conducted by VB are underway and, together with the secondment to
the Project Unit of some staff of the PBs, they are expected to remedy existing
deLiciencies in project appraisal so that staff members of the PBs should be
able to adequately appraise and process subloans under the project without
undue delays.
3.23      Operating policies are similar among the PBs, although the protection
against the risk of excessive lending to an enterprise seems to be better in
the more developed regions. However, the financial situation of those banks
that are less well protected in this respect does not seem to be seriously
affected.
3.24      Appraisal procedures are similar in all banks and show the same
deficiencies as those identified in VB (para. 3.10). Despite these deficien-
cies, all PBs have adequate staff with the necessary background and experience
1/   More detailed data on the banks from the four less developed regions can
be found in Report 364a-YU: Appraisal of an Industrial Credit Project.



- 12
to be able to improve their appraisal techniques with the help of the training
efforts being undertaken (para. 5.09). Supervision of the subborrowers and
procurement and disbursement procedures appear satisfactory.
3.25      All PBs are mixed commercial/investment banks and all (except one
of the member banks of UBZ) are authorized to deal in foreign exchange opera-
tions. For the eight banks long-term loan commitments amounted to close to
30 billion dinars in 1973, of which 7% was for agriculture, but the proportion
of the long-term loans to agriculture varied from 1.3% for the Investment Bank
of Titograd (IBT) to 12% for Kosovska Banka Pristina (KBP) and 28% for VB
(Annex 7, Table 10). In 1972, these eight banks contributed 96% of all bank
investment credits in Yugoslavia for the agricultural sector.
3.26      Short-term resources are essentially used for short-term investments
but prudent usage for long-term investments is also allowed under the control
of the National Bank, and all the banks in the developed regions and the
Privredna Banka Sarajevo (PBS) do so. Short-term loan portfolios account for
16% to 34% of total assets (Annex 7, Table 5).
3.27      The excess of commitments over available resources seems fairly high
and part of the long-term disbursements come from short-term resources (Annex
7, Table 5). The absolute resource gap is even higher since loan applications
may be rejected because of lack of resources. Guarantees are also provided
for suppliers' and other credits.
3.28      Gross incomes less direct financial expenses varied between 1.4% and
1.7% of average total assets in 1973 which is low and reflects both the mixed
nature and the non-profit orientation of the banks. Administrative expenses
in 1973 were between 0.3 and 0.7% of average total assets, which is also low
and shows strict control over the administrative budget. Net income in 1973
was 0.3% to 0.6% of average total assets (Annex 7, Table 3).
3.29      The current ratios were between 0.63:1 and 1.31:1.  Some of the
ratios are low but give no cause for concern since the banks comply with the
stringent liquidity requirements of the National Bank (Annex 7B). The long
term debt/equity ratios were between 3.4:1 and 11.5:1, but when excluding the
loans from socio-political entities, which is very important in the less de-
veloped regions, it is only 1.3:1 to 5.3:1, which is adequate (Annex 7, Table 4).
3.30      It is difficult to evaluate accurately the quality of the invest-
ment portfolios of the banks and the adequacy of their provisions, since data
is incomplete and not always entirely consistent. However, arrears reportedly
account for only 0.6% to 1.7% of the investment portfolios and are always co-
vered by funds for provisions against losses. There seems to be little re-
scheduling, and write offs and payment on guarantees appear negligible, but
a complete judgment on the banks' exposure to bad borrowers cannot be made
without an audit.
3.31      The PBs should be able to meet their repayment obligations to VB
and VB to IBRD for the following reasons:



- 13 -
(a)  they and the governments of their Republics and Provinces are
parties to agreements under the Agricultural Development Plan
that bind them to collaborate in this endeavor.
(b)  the enterprises which are founders of the banks have unlimited
liability toward the banks' obligations and creditors of the
banks have legal recourse to all assets of founders.
(c)  the banks are closely integrated with the economy of their
regions and liquidation is inconceivable.
(d)  the banks have qualified and experienced management and staff,
although some of the banks in the more developed region appear
stronger than the others. Their financial management has been
adequate with no significant losses, arrears have been manage-
able, obligations have been.met satisfactorily and long-term
debt/equity ratios, when excluding loans from socio-political
entities, are adequate.
IV. THE PROJECT
A. Objectives
4.01      The project would provide loans during the three-year investment
period to support the long-term objectives of self-sufficiency in and in-
creased exports of agricultural products. It would make funds available
through VB to a PB in each Republic and Province for credit to social sector
enterprises as well as private farmers, for investments which are needed to
increase livestock, fruit and vegetable production, on-farm mechanization,
irrigation, drainage and land reclamation and agricultural processing facili-
ties.
4.02      The project would help alleviate major constraints to the further
development of agriculture (paras 2.13 and 2.14). It would form part of
Yugoslavia's Agricultural Development Plan and account for about 11% of the
planned investments during the project's three-year investment period. It
would help achieve the Plan's objectives to increase production, improve
product quality, stabilize agricultural development and raise productivity
and income, particularly on private farms. The investments in grain drying
and cold storage would also enable the Government to reduce price fluctuations
by expanding storage facilities. With its emphasis on irrigation and drain-
age, livestock production, fruits and vegetables, the project is expected to
result in significantly reduced crop fluctuations and increased production
of maize, fodder crops, milk, meat, vegetables and fruits. The components
financed by the project are priority investments in line with the Plan's
objectives.



- 14 -
B.   Description
Project Area
4.03      The components financed by the project would be located throughout
Yugoslavia taking into consideration the less developed Republics and .Irov-
inces as well as the less developed regions of the more developed Republics
and Provinces. While the private sector has been relatively neglected in
the past, it would receive, under the project, 43% of the.loan proceeds. Re-
gional and sectoral allocations were agreed upon by the Republics and Prov-
inces taking into account the agricultural development priorities, regional
income and sectoral distribution objectives and will be enforced by VB and
the PBs (Annex 4). The proposed IBRD loan allocation is summarized below:

28.5 (57%)    21.5 (43%)    50.0    100.0
Livestock Production
4.04      Investments by social sector organizations and private farmers would
finance expansion of existing livestock production by either modification to
existing buildings or construction of new buildings, milking parlors, and other
physical facilities for milk production, cattle fattening, and sheep rearing.
Provision would be made for the purchasing of the basic herd as part of the
investment costs. Also, engineering services for design, construction assist-
ance and training would be included in social sector projects. Construction
of the livestock facilities for each investment would be phased over two years.
Production would be largely for the domestic market with some meat intended
for export.
4.05      Dairy Farms.  For the social sector, the project would provide for
the purchase of about 5,600 in-calf heifers and dairy cows, primarily Simmental
and Friesian breeds, of which about 1,400 head would be imported. Calves would
be sold at about four months of age to heifer rearing and calf fattening units.
Semen from Government artificial insemination centers would be used for breed-
ing. For the private sector, the project would provide for the purchase of
about 13,000 in-calf heifers and dairy cows. In addition about 5,500 cull
cows would be replaced annually with in-calf heifers to maintain full capacity.



- 15 -
4.06      Cattle Fattening Farms.  The project would provide for the construc-
tion of facilities in the social sector to feed at one time about 7,500 head
of young cattle. Feeder calves would be purchased at about 220 kg and after
feeding for about 200 days, they would be sold at 450 kg. Feed would consist
primarily of formulated concentrate mixtures. Straw would be used for bed-
ding. Building design would facilitate easy cleaning of pens with a tractor-
maounted scraper. Feeding troughs would be served with a side delivery trail-
er. Each year some 13,500 cattle would be fattened for slaughter.
4.07      Sheep Farms.  Subloans would be provided to private sector sheep
farmers to increase their flocks by purchasing breeding stock. Provisions
would include purchasing of about 55,000 ewes, construction of winter housing,
and feeding facilities, and pasture improvement to extend the grass season and
to increase hay production for winter feeding.
Vegetahle Production
4.08      Subloans would be provided to social sector enterprises to develop
about 4,200 ha for commercial vegetable production to supply mainly peas,
tomatoes, green beans and peppers for processing.  Included would be on-farm
irrigation faAilities and mechanization primarily for harvesting and plastic
green houses. Production of early peas would allow for a second crop such as
green beans or maize silage.
4.09      Subloans would be providled to private farmers for development of
about 1,000 ha for labor intensive mixed vegetable production and about 40 ha
of plastic green houses for early vegetable production. Included in the
project for mixed vegetable production would be pumps, pipelines, wells and
other equipakent to construct small-scale on-farm irrigation systems. Also,
included would be tubing, plastic for covering, and other items for construc-
tion of greern houses. Increased vegetable production would mainly supply the
domestic market. Some early and processed vegetables would be exported.
Fru.:t Production
4.10      Subloans would be provided in the project to private farmers for
on-farm development of about 400 ha of orchards, of about 30 ha of soft fruit
plantings and of about 850 ha of vineyards. Included would be such fruit
production for which there exists strong domestic or export demand, such as
raspberries, strawberries, cherries, peaches, apples, black currants and
grapes. Investments would include planting materials, fertilizers, labor,
land preparation, supports, wire and labor.
Land Reclamation
4.11      Subloans would be provided in the project to social sector enter-
s to finance about 7,600 ha of on-farm irrigation systems, about 8,400
ha of open and closed field drainage systems and about 9,800 ha of land dev-
elopment. Included would be provision for engineering services for design and
construction assistance. Construction of the land reclamation components of
the project would be phased over two years.



- 16 -
4.12      Social Sector Irrigation.  Investments under the project would be
for sprinkler irrigation systems. The system output design would depend on
the expected precipitation and the water demand during vegetative period
which varies significantly throughout the country. Provision would be in-
cluded for purchasing sprinkler lines, pumping facilities, main lines, pipe
installation, labor outlet valves, and miscellaneous avcillary equipment.
4.13      Social Sector Drainage.  Investments under the project would be for
sub-surface closed field drainage, sub-surface and surface field drainage,
pumping stations, land preparation, access roads, and outlet drains (main,
secondary, an.l tertiary). Included would be provisions for purchasing pipe,
pumps, and ancillary equipment and for construction of camber bedding, gravel
trench drains, and water removal drains.
4.14      Social Sector Land Development.  Investments under the project would
be for clearing land of brush, trees, and hedges, levelling land to eliminate
nonfunctionaL physical features, functional drainage, access roads, and con-
solidation of small parcels of land into large plots for efficient production.
Provisions would be included for machine services, surveying and pumping,
relocation of facilities, fertilizers, protective agents and miscellaneous
ancillary equipment.
4.15      Private Sector Land Development.  Subloans would be provided in the
project to private farmers to finance about 1,200 ha of land development.
Investments would be for clearing land, land levelling, drainage, on-farm
irrigation, access roads and consolidation of land. Included would be survey-
ing to establish boundaries, machine services, fertilizers, protective agents,
and consolidation of small parcels into larger plots to increase productivity
through better land utilization and mechanization. At full development, an-
nual income would about double to 23,000 Dinars per 5 ha farm.
Agricultural-Industries
4.16      Subloans would be provided to the social sector enterprises for the
construction of buildings and other physical facilities and the purchase of
equipment and machinery for grain drying facilities, cold storage plants
and milk collecting stations. Construction of the agro-industries components
of the project would be phased over two years.
4.17      Grain Drying Facilities.  In Yugoslavia to obt.ain maximum yields,
late maturing hybrid maizes have been introduced. During years with normal
rainfall, the kernel moisture content ranges between 28% to 32% and in wet
years, it reaches 34% or more. To avoid excessive losses in quality (feed
value) and quantity (up to 17%), the moisture content has to be reduced to
14% or below within 40 days of harvest. The project would reduce the present
shortag. of drying capacity in providing for the construction of about 320,000
tons of annual drying capacity which would be primarily for maize drying but
would also be used for other cereals, particularly in wet years. Subloans
would te provided for direct fired dryers with a drying capacity from 22 to
32 tonim per hour. The facilities would be located adjacent to grain elevators
with a minimum storage capacity of 20,000 tons. Included would be provisions



- 17 -
for the construction of access roads, for the purchase of fuel tanks, and
handling equipment. Provisions would also be made for engineering services
during construction and technical training. At full operation, about 32,000
tons of grain usually lost because of spoilage would be available to the eco-
non.y.
4.18      Cold Storage Facilities.  Subloans would be provided for the con-
struction of about 6,800 tons of cold storage capacity. Included would be
provisions for processing and freezing fresh vegetables and fruit. The
facilities would be single story structures divided into refrigerated chambers
for storing fruit and vegetables and non-refrigerated rooms for processing.
Included would be equipment for handling, cleaning, processing, packaging
and freezing products. Provision would also be included for engineering
services during design and construction.
4.19      Milk Collection Stations.  Investments would be provided in the
project for the construction of about 240 milk collection statiors each with
a minimum capacity of 2,000 liters. The building would be a simple single
story structure designed to house two insulated refrigerated milk cooling
tanks and ancillary equipment. Included would be filters, pumps, piping and
sampling equipment for collecting small quantities for butter fat and quality
testing. All equipment contacting milk would be constructed of stainless
steel or other acceptable materials meeting international sanitary standards.
On-Farm Mechanization
4.20      Mechanization and the use of fertilizers and protective agents
would be the most effective measures to improve the productivity of the
private sector. Subloans would be provided in the project to private farmers
to finance about 400 on-farm investments in mechanization as illustrated in
the model Annex 8, Appendix 20. Included would be provisions to purchase
about 400 tractors and implements such as trailers, ploughs and rollers.
Other larger implements would be jointly procured with farmers who own or
also buy tractors. Farmers with tractors would cultivate their own farms
and sell machine services to neighboring farmers who do not own tractors.
Investments would be phased over a two year implementation period.
C. Cost Estimates

of which 20% or Din 430 million (US$25 million) would be foreign exchange.
Details of investment costs are shown in Annex 8 and are summarized below.

4.24      The IBRD loan of US$50 million provides for the minimum participa-
tion necessary to establish Bank leverage in achieving the Project's important
policy and institutional objectives and to have meaningful financial par-
ticipations in each PB and each subsector. It would be made to VB for 15
years, including four years of grace, for on-lending to sub-borrowers through
a PB in each Republic and Province (paras 5.06-5.08). The project cash flow
(Annex 9) shows that sub-borrowers' repayments would generate sufficient in-
flow to repay the IBRD loan within 15 years and to cover a reasonable degree
of slippage In repayment. For the social sector, 30% of the total project
cost would be contributed by sub-borrowers and 30% by the PBs. For the pri-
vate sector, 20% of the total project cost would be contributed by sub-
borrowers and 40% from the PBs. The remaining 40% would be provided by the
proposed IBRD loan. Agreement was obtained during negotiations on the afore-
said conditions.



- 20 -
E. Procurement
International Procurement
4.25      International competitive bidding according to IBRD Guidelines, and
under the supervision of VB, would be applied by the sub-borrowers for the
procurement of cold storage facilities and the planting and harvesting equip-
ment for the social sector vegetable subprojects (US$7 million). The cold
storage facilities would be procured under a turnkey contract since the
processing units need to be integrated with the freezer and chill storage
units. Domestic manufacturers would receive a 15% margin of preference or
the actual level of import duty, whichever is less, in bid comparisons.
4.26      About 1,400 in-calf heifers (US$2 million) for the social sector
dairy farm component would be procured from abroad through solicitation of
bids from suppliers from at least three countries. VB would maintain a con-
solidated summary for each procurement of imported heifers showing the bids
received and selected together with a copy of the contract awarded to the
successful bidder.
Local Procurement
4.27      The facilities for dairy and cattle fattening farms, milk collection
stations and grain drying (US$36 million) are simple structures, widely dis-
persed throughout the country, and consist mainly of civil works. They would
be most suitable for local contractors and would be procured under local
bidding procedures satisfactory to the Bank.
4.28      Items for on-farm development (US$54 million) would not be suitable
for bulk procurement and international competitive bidding because of phasing
of subprojects and the dispersion of investors throughout the country. Sub-
borrowers would purchase equipment, fertilizers, seeds, livestock, small
quantities of construction materials, pipe and planting materials directly
through existing commercial channels which include both domestic and interna-
tional suppliers or through local bidding procedures already determined to be
satisfactory to the Bank. Machinery and equipment dealers maintain adequate
supplies of spare parts, provide servicing and repair facilities and are suit-
ably competitive. During negotiations assurances were obtained that the pro-
curement procedures contained in paragraphs 4.25 through 4.28 would be followed.
F. Disbursements
4.29      IBRD disbursements would take about 4 years although the entire
loan is expected to be committed by the end of year 2. The annual phasing
of investments and the forecast of quarterly disbursements are shown in Annex



- 21 -
6. Disbursement of the IBRD loan would be made against 57% of the social
sector and 50% of the private sector subloan amounts disbursed by VB to the
PBs (para 4.24). Disbursement requests for the social sector investments
would be supported by full documentation. For subloans to the private sector,
the Bank would disburse against certificates of expenditure. The same proce-
dures would apply for disbursement by VB to the PBs. Supporting documents
and contracts-would be retained by the PBs and would be available for review
by VB and IBRD during supervision.
G. Environmental Impact
4.30      The project is not expected to have significant adverse environmental
impact on the project area.
V. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A. Organization
The Federal Coordination Committee
5.01      A committee to coordinate agricultural investments in Yugoslavia,
but with no actual "line" responsibilities, has been established under the
Agricultural Development Plan. Its members are representatives of the
Secretariats for Agriculture of the Republics and Autonomous Provinces and
of the Federal Government with the Deputy Chairman of the Federal Committee
for Agriculture (i.e. the Ministry of Agriculture) as chairman. Assurances
were obtained during negotiations that the Committee would continue to per-
form its coordination role with respect to the project including the respons-
ibility for policy questions and reallocation of proceeds of the loan between
Republics and Provinces and sectors.
The Bank of Vojvodina
5.02      A summary appraisal of VB, the borrower and apex bank for the proj-
ect, is in paras 3.08 to 3.19. VB's organization, financial condition and
operating policies are adequate for an efficient implementation of the proj-
ect.
5.03      A project unit has been established in VB under the name of "Project
Operations Department". Assurances were obtained during negotiations that
the Department would continue to operate in VB, that it would be adequately
staffed and responsible for all subloans under the project. VB would submit
to the Bank for its approval an organizational and staff reinforcement pro-
gram (Annex 7F) and would staff the Department accordingly.



- 22 -
5.04      As the borrower and apex bank, VB would be responsible for the
repayment of the proposed loan and the proper execution of the project. It
would ensure that the policies and procedures of sublending under the project,
as outlined below, would be followed by the PBs and that the arrangements for
procurement, disbursement, audit and monitoring referred to in paras 4.25-
4.29 and 5.24 - 5.26 would be enforced.
5.05      It is estimated that the cost of operations of the Project Opera-
tions Department would be about Din 10 million over the three year project
implementation period. VB's risk on its loans to the PBs is negligible and
VB is not expected to make a profit on its loans to the PBs. To help meet
the cost of its operations in administering the project, VB would receive one
percentage point in interest rate spread on IBRD funds onlent. Therefore, VB's
lending rate on its loans to PBs out of the proceeds of the Bank loan would
be 9.5%.
The Participating Banks
5.06      One bank from each Republic and Province (listed in Annex 7 D, para
35) would participate under the project and VB would function as a PB for the
Autonomous Province of Vojvodina. A summary appraisal of the PBs is given in
paras 3.20 to 3.31. Assurances were obtained during negotiations that VB
would be informed and, in turn, would inform the Bank, prior to any substan-
tial changes in the financial conditions and the operating policies of the
PBs, that would affect the implementation of the project.
5.07      A subsidiary loan agreement will be entered between each PB and VB
covering the policies and procedures of sublending under the project, project
related staffing of the PBs, and the arrangements for procurement, disbursement,
audit and monitoring. Signing of the seven agreements, satisfactory to the
Bank, between VB and the PBs would be a condition of effectiveness of the Bank
loan. VB, in its function as PB for Vojvodina, would follow the same sub-
lending, procurement, disbursement, audit and monitoring procedures. Assur-
ances to this effect were obtained during negotiations.
5.08      The PBs, including VB, would be directly responsible for the execu-
tion of the project in their respective Republic or Province. A PB will nor-
mally finance, under the project, only subprojects in its respective Republic
or Province. Each PB would receive from VB a subloan out of the proceeds of
the Bank loan according to its allocation (para 4.03). The PBs would present
appropriate documentation for reimbursement of Bank portion of the subloans
to VB for review and submission to the Bank for disbursement. The grace and
repayment period would be such that each repayment date of the suibloan to VB
would be three months before the corresponding repayment date by VB to the
Bank. The interest rate to the PBs would be 9.5% and the commitment fee 0.75%.
These provisions would be covered under the subsidiary loan agreements.



- 23 -
Training
5.09      A seminar in appraisal criteria and techniques used in Bank agricul-
ture credit projects was held in January 1975 to train VB and PB staff and,
at VB's request, a Bank staff member participated. The Bank will assist fur-
ther in training VB staff once the staff reinforcement program has been
designed and agreed. Training of the staff of the PBs will be the responsi-
bility of the staff of the Project Operations Department.
B. Policies and Procedures of Projects Sublending
praisal Criteria of Subprojects
5.10      In appraising subprojects in the social sector, VB and the PBs
would ensure that:
(a) subprojects are technically feasibile, financially viable,
commercially sound and economically justified, and markets
and processing facilities for their incremental production
are available;
(b) subprojects will generate sufficient cash flow to cover
debt service, legal claims, compulsory allocations to
the enterprise's funds and reinvestment needs and achieve
adequate financial rates of return, with a particular
justification required if the financial rate of return
is below 12%;
(c) enterprises borrowing for subprojects have a satisfactory
financial structure, especially an adequate debt/equity
ratio of normally less than 3:1. Exceptions may be allowed
(based on the financial and economic merit of the subproject)
provided that the enterprise enters into an agreement with a
bank to increase its equity over a suitable period of time
to not less than 25% of its total capitalization;
(d) appropriate consideration is given to the need for
introduction of capital-intensive, automated technology
as opposed to more labor intensive methods and to the con-
sequences of such a choice on the investment costs of the
subprojects.
5.11      Subprojects in the private sector would normally be submitted to
the PBs by social sector enterprises, kombinats or cooperatives which having
aggregated investment proposals of several private farmers with whom they have
entered into a cooperation agreement would borrow on their behalf. However,
private farmers may also submit directly investment proposals as subprojects
to a PB. In appraising subprojects in the private sector, VB and the PBs
would satisfy themselves that:



- 24 -
(a)  subprojects are technically feasible, financially viable,
commercially sound and economically justified, and markets
and processing facilities for their incremental production
are available;
(b)  subprojects would generate sufficient cash flow to cover
debt service, legal claims and needs for reinvestment;
(c)  adequate and continuing technical assistance, and suitable
market outlets at fair and reasonable prices are available
to the private farmers;
(d)  in the case of lending through social sector organizations,
the cooperation agreements are adequate.
Assurances were obtained during negotiations that procedures as outlined in
paras 5.10 and 5.11 would be followed by VB and the PBs in appraising sub-
projects.
The Subloans
5.12      The social sector sub-borrowers would provide a minimum of 30% of
investment costs and private sector sub-borrowers would provide a minimum of
20% of investment costs. The contribution of private farmers can be in the
form of their own labor or of building materials. In no case would the non-
cash contribution exceed 20% of investment costs. For this purpose, the
private farmer's own family's labor would be evaluated by using the standard
number of working hours and the wage rate for unskilled agricultural workers
in the social sector in the same area. The private farmer's building mater-
ials would be valued at their replacement costs.
5.13      The PBs would provide a maximum of 30% of investment costs in
the case of social sector sub-borrowers and a maximum of 40% of investment
costs in the case of private sector sub-borrowers. The remaining 40% of
investment costs would come from the Bank loan and would be disbursed through
VB to PBs except for Vojvodina. Prior to disbursing the Bank Loan, VB would
assure the availability of counterpart funds and sub-borrowers' contribution
for each specific subproject. Assurances to this effect were obtained during
negotiations.
5.14      Seasonal credit would be provided by the PBs or the respective
commercial banks of sub-borrowers where appropriate. Assurances were ob-
tained during negotiations that VB would, prior to requesting disbursement of
the Bank Loan, be satisfied that the necessary amount of seasonal credit would
be available for each subproject.
Lending Terms
5.15      The terms of subloans under the project would be related to the
cash flow projections of the investments (Annex 8). Assurances were obtained

during negotiations that the grace and repayment periods on the subloans will
not exceed the following:

Interest Rates
5.16      As detailed in Chapter 3, interest rates for investments in agricul-
ture vary between 3% and 6% for the private sector and 3% and 12% for the
social sector, depending upon the Republic or Province, the source of funds
and the type of investment and they are often revised every year. Most of
the counterpart funds under the project would be provided by the Federal,
Republican and Provincial budgets or entrusted to the PBs by producers'
associations and similar organizations.
5.17      The interest rates under the Project will be:
-    on the portion of a subloan financed out of the proceeds of
the Bank loan 11%;
-    on the portion of a subloan financed out of a local funds, the
rate prevailing at that time, in that location, for that type
of investment.
These rates are expected to result in equivalent blend rates between 7.5% and
11.5% for the social sector (average 9.75%) and between 7% and 8.5% for the
private sector (average 7.75%). The rate structure would provide the PBs
with a spread of 1.5% on the proceeds of the Bank loan and usually at least
0.5% on the local funds portion of the subloans. In those cases where the
spreads would not be sufficient to cover their costs of administering the
Project, the PBs would meet their deficits from their overall net revenues.
As part of the project, a study would be carried out by VB to analyze the pre-
sent interest rate structure for the agricultural sector and to determine
whether uniform interest rates throughout the country for all types of invest-
ments would be effective and feasible. Assurances were obtained during nego-
tiations that the study would be carried out within one year of loan signing
under terms of reference acceptable to the Bank (Annex 7G).



- 26 -
5.18      Assurances were obtained that the introduction of foreign exchange
risk arrangements satisfactory to the Bank would be a condition of effective-
ness. It is expected that the social sector sub-borrowers would carry the
full foreign exchange risk with respect to the portion of subloans financed
out of the proceeds of the Bank loan for their own investments. Other suit-
able arrangements would be made for the foreign exchange risk on subloans
made to private farmers. It is also expected that VB and the PBs would be
protected against the exchange risk on the foreign exchange surplus accruing
to them from subloan repayments occurring earlier than amortization payments
of the Bank loan.
Appraisal Procedures of Subprojects
5.19      Investment proposals would be submitted by enterprises to the PB
in their Republic or Province. Several investment proposals in the private
sector would usually be aggregated and submitted by a social sector organiza-
tion. Private farmers may also submit directly a proposal to a PB. The PBs
would appraise the subprojects according to the criteria set forth in paras
5.10 and 5.11 and the PBs would register each subproject with VB.
5.20      The following procedures would be applied:
(a)  the PBs would approve all subprojects having an investment
cost below US$1 million;
(b)  VB would review the appraisal of and approve all subprojects
having an investment cost between US$1 million and US$1.5
million;
(c)  the Bank would review the appraisal of and approve all subproj-
ects having an investment cost above US$1.5 million.
Apart from the subprojects costing more than US$1.5 million, the Bank would
also review and approve the appraisal of the first social as well as private
sector subprojects of each PB and of VB and all cattle fattening subprojects
(para 6.02). Thus, the Bank would review about 50 subprojects totalling $38
million or 30% of total project cost. The approval of the first subprojects
will be a prerequisite for disbursement through each PB (and VB). Assurances
were obtained during negotiations that these procedures would be followed.
C. Project Monitoring
Audit
5.21      Assurances were obtained during negotiations that VB and all
PBs would each maintain a separate account for the project. This would in-
clude disbursements made under the project for all subloans.



- 27 -
5.22      The accounts of Yugoslav banks are examined, once a year, by the
Social Accounting Service (SAS), which verifies the cash balances and conducts
a limited review of the financial statements for mathematical accuracy and
comparisons with previous years. A more detailed field examination is con-
ducted at irregular intervals. The main purpose of the SAS examination is
to ensure that financial transactions are in compliance with the law. The
methods used, while meeting the standards applicable in Yugoslavia, do not
meet generally accepted international auditing standards. The absence of
a proper audit of the banks should not be considered critical to the consid-
eration of their creditworthiness; VB is considered a suitable borrower for
the proposed loan, and all the PBs appear capable of carrying out the project
efficiently. However, uncertainties about the quality of the banks' portfolios
must be recognized as looming large in evaluating their financial soundess.
In the future, audits undertaken according to international standards should
become an essential part of the information system available to the banks'
management and founders, to the Government, to the Bank, and to prospective
foreign lenders.
5.23      In January 1974 the SAS initiated, with the assistance of Coopers
and Lybrand, a UK auditing firm, a program for training some of its staff in
modern auditing. This training successfully oriented a pioneer group of SAS
staff towards internationally accepted audit practice. With Coopers and
Lybrand's assistance, SAS is now preparing a second phase in which trainees
from the first phase will carry on with their practical training and set up
a training program in Yugoslavia for other SAS staff.
5.24      Auditing the Yugoslav banks poses special problems because of their
size and diversity of operations. Audit work on the Privredna Banka Sarajevo,
a Borrower under the Industrial Credit Project (Loan 1013-YU), began in 1974.
During 1975 this work will be continued and work on the Stopanska Banka Skopje,
a borrower under the Macedonia Agroindustries Project (Loan 894-YU) and the
Industrial Credit Project (Loan 1012-YU) is expected to begin. In view of
the limited numbers of trained SAS staff, audit work on the two banks will
coilcentrate on management control and procedures and matters specifically
related to IBRD projects. Precise details are still under discussion between
the Bank and the SAS. If the SAS is unable to carry out satisfactory audits
alone, they will seek, through an interim association, the assistance of an
internationally reputable auditing firm, acceptable to the Bank. Assurances
were obtained during negotiations that VB's accounts would be audited under
the same arrangements as under the Industrial Credit Project starting with
the 1975 accounts, and it is expected that the accounts of the other PBs (in-
cluding the five member banks of UBZ) would also be audited in accordance with
a schedule to be agreed upon after discussions with the SAS and the auditing
firm training the SAS staff. The subsidiary loan agreement would provide
that during the transition period, i.e., before satisfactory audits can be
performed, the PBs would submit through VB to the Bank, SAS certified finan-
cial statements acceptable to the Bank, within six months of the end of the
fiscal year. The subsidiary loan agreement would also provide that, when
sntisfactory audits can be performed, financial statements audited in accord-
ance with international standard would be submitted by the PBs through VB to
the Bank within six months of the end of each fiscal year.



- 28 -
Supervision
5.25      Assurances were obtained during negotiations that VB and the PBs
would ensure that adequate technical and financial supervision of on-going
subprojects would be carried out. The social sector sub-borrowers and the
social sector enterprises borrowing on behalf of private farmers, would
submit to the respective PB, SAS-certified financial statements within six
months from the end of each fiscal year. The basic supervision would be done
by the PBs under the general responsibility of VB.
Reporting Requirements
5.26      A project monitoring system would be designed by VB to provide the
Bank with quarterly and annual reports covering the progress of the project,
sublending operations under the project, financial data pertaining to the
project, the financial statements of VB and the PBs and general data on agri-
culture and economic policy affecting the implementation of the project. VB
would also be responsible for evaluating the project's benefits. During ne-
gotiations, assurances were obtained to this effect.
VI. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
A. Production, Markets and Prices
Production
6.01      The following table shows the incremental annual production of
main crops generated by the project (Annex 8):
Annual Production on Project Farms
Crop                                 Pre-Project   Full Development  Incremental
'---------------'000 Tons--------------

Maize Silage                              55.0           200.0           145.0
