mated at Din 1,135 million (US$66.8 million) at November 1974 prices.



- 29 -
Markets (Annex 3, paras. 19-34)
6.02      The incremental production derived from the project would serve the
following purposes: additional maize, alfalfa and silage production would
result in a much needed increase in livestock feed supplies and help reduce
imports of feed grains. Increased sugar beet production would help utilize
full capacity of the existing processing facilities and reduce net imports of
sugar. Incremental vegetable production would be needed predominantly to
supply increasing domestic demand while the additional fruit production would
equally help promote exports as well as satisfy rising domestic needs. The
increase in milk production would allow the extremely low level of per capita
consumption to rise (Annex 3, Table 14). Increased beef meat production would
supply both the domestic and foreign, particularly Western European markets.
With the strong demand for project commodities largely in the domestic markets,
the incremental production at full development (less than 1% of production for
maize, 3% of production for sugar beets, 2% of production for alfalfa and
silage, 10% of production for vegetables, 1% of production for fruits, 3% of
production for milk and 1.4% of production for beef meat) would not pose any
particular marketing problem, except possibly for the cattle fattening component
(Annex 3, paras 27-31). The export beef market at this time appears uncertain
and difficult to project. During negotiations assurances were obtained from
VB that the appraisals of cattle fattening subprojects would include a detail-
ed export market analysis and that these subprojects be submitted to the Bank
for approval, irrespective of the free limit arrangements outlined in para
5.20.
Prices
6.03      In Yugoslavia, farmgate prices as well as farm input prices with
few exceptions have, in practice, been determined by demand and supply. The
Government's efforts to contain increasing prices have failed largely because
of the inadequate price control mechanisms (Annex 2, para 14). The incre-
mental production from the project, which would result in substituting for
or reducing the growth of imports, or supplying otherwise not satisfied needs,
would not induce a reduction in producer prices since the expected demand in-
crease is likely to exceed the additional production. Thus rising farmgate
prices due to excess demand are expected, but they would increase less than
without the project.
B. Financial Benefits
6.04      The financial results for typical investment models under the
project are given in Annex 8. The calculations are based on November 1974
Yugoslav prices for both inputs and outputs, which are generally in line with
international prices. The financial rates of return of the models vary be-
tween 14% and 32% (Annex 8). The sensitivity analysis yields the following:
if investment costs were raised by 10% the financial rates of return would
vary between 12% and 30%; an increase in operating expenses by 10% would
result in financial rates of return between 0% and 30%; and if revenues were
to drop by 10% the rates would range from -3% to 27%.



- 30 -
6.05      Some investments are extremely sensitive to an increase in operating
costs or decrease in revenues, notably those in cattle fattening. As men-
ioned above (para 6.02), these subprojects would be reviewed by the Bank to
ensure their financial viability. The financial rates of return of the other
investment models have remained satisfactory under sensitivity testing, as it
can be expected that inflationary price rises would affect both costs and
benefits and tend to maintain the present relationship between them. In any
case subprojects yielding financial rates of return of less than 12% would
not be approved by VB and the PBs (para. 5.10).
6.06      About 380 social sector enterprises and 10,200 private sector farm-
ers, of which about 3170 (or 31%) in the less developed areas, would benefit
directly from the project. The average annual income of social sector enter-
prises would increase from Din 214,000 to Din 737,000 (or from US$14,120 to
US$43,350), affecting the income of more than 3,000 families. In the private
sector, a farmer's family's average annual income would increase from Din
10,250 to Din 27,450 (or from US$600 to US$1,600). In addition, the project
would, through the investments in milk collection stations and cold storage
facilities, benefit indirectly another 46,400 small farms by raising their
incomes by an average of about Din 1,700 a year.
C. Economic Benefits
Foreign Exchange Earnings
6.07      The project in concentrating on the domestic market would, over its
20 year lifetime, generate only about $115 million in foreign exchange through
increased exports. Taking into account foreign exchange savings through
import substitution (mainly feed grains and milk products) and direct and in-
direct import components of investments and operating costs, the total net
foreign exchange earnings or savings, over the project's 20 year lifetime,
are estimated to amount to the order of US$200 million (at 1974 prices).
Employment
6.08      The project would generate substantial employment opportunities.
An estimated 2,650 incremental man-years would be generated from investments
in the social and about 3,500 man-years in the private sector. For an addi-
tional 10,200 farmers, the project would reduce underemployment and provide
means to use more fully their free capacities in buildings.
6.09      About 8,700 additional man-years would be generated during the 3
year investment period: 6,700 man-years in the social sector for building
and construction work and engineering services, and 2,000 man-years in the
private sector for expanding buildings, establishing fruit trees and vine-
yards, and digging wells. The additional investments and consumption of in-
puts, such as fertilizer, pesticides, fuel, via their backward linkages, and
the increased production via forward linkages, would generate sizeable in-
direct employment opportunities, which were not quantified.



- 31 -
Income Distribution
6.10      A significant share of the project's financing would benefit the
poorer farmers. About US$19 million or 38% of the proposed Bank loan would
directly and indirectly benefit an estimated 38,800 (of which about 26% in
less developed regions) small farmers or more than two-thirds of all private
farmers affected by the project. They have incomes significantly below those
of unskilled workers in the social sector.
Resource Allocation
6.11      The project in involving eight major banks would bring about sub-
stantial benefits from improved resource allocation, particularly in the
less developed regions. The staff of VB and the PBs, which would be trained
under this project, is likely to handle most of the credit financed invest-
ments in the agricultural sector. Their experience gained under the project
could result in significant savings of investment resources in the agricul-
tural sector.
Rate of Return
6.12      The economy is expected to realize a return of 27% from total proj-
ect investments, including physical contingencies (Annex 10). Although the
administrative and training costs are included in the project costs, their
benefits cannot be quantified. Hired labor has been valued at prevailing
wage rates. Family labor has not been costed as operating expenses because
there are unused labor capacities on private farms. Wheat, maize, feed
grains and beef were projected at world market price levels. Other com-
modities were valued at November 1974 farmgate prices, adjusted for import
duties, taxes and subsidies. A sensitivity analysis was applied to test the
project against changes in investment costs, operating expenses and produc-
tion results. By increasing investment costs and operating expenses alter-
rnatively by 10%, the rate of return would drop to 24% and 21%, respectively.
By decreasing the value of the benefits by 10%, the overall return would drop
to 18% (Annex 10).
VII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
7.01      During negotiations, agreement was reached on the following princi-
pal issues:
The Borrower will
(i) adequately staff and continue to operate its "Project Operations
Department" as its unit responsible for all subloans under the
project (para 5.03);
(ii) follow the procedures concerning subsidiary loan agreements
as outlined in para 5.07;



- 32 -
(iii) ensure that subprojects be appraised, reviewed and approved
according to the procedures and criteria as outlined in paras
5.10, 5.11, 5.19 and 5.20.
7.02      Conditions for making the proposed loan effective are the signing
of the seven agreements between VB and the PBs, satisfactory to the Bank, and
covering items as outlined in para 5.08 and the introduction of a foreign
exchange risk protection scheme satisfactory to the Bank (para. 5.18).
7.03      Subject to the condition of effectiveness described above, the
project would be suitable for a Bank loan of US$50 million, equivalent, for
a term of 15 years including a four year grace period.



ANNEX 1
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
AN AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
The Agricultural Sector
Geography and Climate
1.        The Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia is located between
latitudes 400 and 470 north and longitudes 13 and 230 east. As a federal
state it consists of six Socialist Republics: Bosnia-Herzegovina, Croatia,
Macedonia, Montenegro, Serbia Proper and Slovenia: and two Autonomous Prov-
inces attached to Serbia: Vojvodina and Kosovo. It borders Italy on the
west, Austria, Hungary and Romania on the north, and Bulgaria, Greece and
Albania on the east. The southern border runs along Greece, Albania and
the Adriatic coast.
2.        Of Yugoslavia's total territory of 255,804 km2 about 58% is recorded
as agricultural land while the rest is taken up by forests, rivers and lakes.
Of the agricultural land about 70% is arable and the rest permanent pastures.
The extremely fertile soils predominantly located in Vojvodina and the north-
ern regions of Croatia represent only one-sixth of the country's total area
but contain nearly a third of the arable land and produce more than half of
the country's output of wheat and maize and over three-quarters of its sugar
beet. This region is also suitable for intensive livestock and vegetable
production. In the hills (land up to 500 meters in altitude) accounting for
about 40% of the total area and covering much of Serbia, Slovenia and Bosnia
a mixed farm pattern prevails. Important lines of nroduction are livestock
raising and fruit growing and, to a lesser degree, farm crops. The mountain
region covering 44% of the country is generally unfavorable for crop produc-
tion, but extensive upland pastures make possible sheen and some cattle rais-
ing.  At the Adriatic coast, vineyards and some olive and citrus groves are
found, whereas in southern Macedonia sub-tropical crops, such as tobacco,
cotton and rice are grown.
3.        In the valleys and plains alluvial soils are predominant with
Cernozem prevailing in most of Vojvodina and the northeastern part of Croatia.
Delluvial and terra rossa soils are common to the hills. Soil erosion is a
major problem on steep, barren hills and mountain slopes. Cultivated soils
are usually well supplied with organic matter and potash, but poor in phos-
phates. The pH ranges in most areas from acid to neutral; slightly alkaline
soils are located in the northern part of the country.
4.        There are three basic climatic zones in Yugoslavia:  (i) the Mediter-
ranean, (ii) the modified Atlantic, and (iii) the Continental climatic zones.
Due to the topographic configuration of the country these zonal climates are



ANNEX 1
Page 2
much modified by the terrain. The Mediterranean climate of the Adriatic
coast has mild winters and hot summer (annual average temperature 15C),
with relatively heavy rainfall (e.g. Dubrovnik 1,500 mm), characteristically
in the autumn and winter months while the summers are dry. The modified
Atlantic climate of Slovenia and northwestern Croatia is the coldest in the
country (annual average 9C), with heavy rainfall (e.g. Ljubljana 1,546 mm),
which is lower in winter, and relatively even through the other sec.sons. The
Continental climate is most characteristic of Vojvodina. Here, too, the
winters are very cold, but the summers are quite hot. Precipitation in this
area is among the lowest in the country (e.g. Vrsac 682 mm), but is charac-
teristically heaviest in late spring and early summer, which allows high
plant utilization in the period of greatest growth. A temperate continental
climate exists between the Continental and Mediterranean zones, covering
essentially the whole of the Dinaric System, which has the lowest annual
average temperatures, with cold winters and cool summers, and heavy precipi-
tation (e.g. Kolasin 2,670 mm) concentrated in the autumn and winter. Another
transitional zone also exists, which includes most of  'acedonia, where the
Continental influence is basic and the 'Tediterranean secondary.
Agriculture in the Economy
4.        In Yugoslavia almost 407 of the population still lives in rural
areas. Agriculture accounts for 20% of the social product, constitutes
roughly 15-20% of total commodity exports and emplovs about 44% of the
labor force of Yugoslavia. Though its role has been declining relatively
with rapid industrialization it still remains a sector of basic importance
in the overall development of the economy. Yugoslav agricultural policy 1/
has passed through various phases. After the abandonment of collectivisa-
tion in 1953, the government concentrated on developing agricultural marketed
production through a modern social sector incorporating the earlier state
farms and agricultural cooperatives which replaced the collective farms as
the main basis for providing the marketed output for a rapidly growing non-
agricultural population. In social sector agriculture heavv investments
were made to modernize and mechanize production, to increase the use of
chemical inputs and to expand irrigation. The private sector was relatively
neglected bv Government policy which placed emphasis on voluntary cooperation
between peasant farmers and the social sector agriculture as the major device
for increasing production in the private sector. The social product in agri-
culture increased at an average rate of 3.2% per year during 1968-73, with
the social sector output increasing at about 5.2% per year and the private
sector at about 2.4% per year. 2/
1/   For a discussion of agricultural development policies see Annex 2.
2/   For a discussion of agricultural production and marketing see Annex 3.



ANNEX 1
Page 3
Organization of the Agricultural Sector
5.        One of the important characteristic features of Yugoslav agricul-
ture is the senarate existence of two sectors i.e. social and private with
different farm structures. The social sector has its origin in the postwar
collectivization policy, and received further impetus by the Agrarian Reform
of 1953, which limited the size of private or individual holding to 10 hec-
tares of cultivable land or 25 hectares of forest land, and brought 276,000
hectares of additional land under the social sector. Since then, the land
resources of the social sector have almost doubled due to reclamation, pur-
chase and lease from private holders. The social sector in 1973 held 15%
of the cultivated land, employed 6% of total agricultural manpower, produced
33% of the agricultural product and 45% of the market production. In 1973
there were 1,745 social sector holdings averaging 1,247 hectares of culti-
vated area per holding.

It is evident that while the share of the social sector has generally in-
creased, the private sector continues to maintain its importance in maize,
frult and milk products (Table 1). However, the private sector's role in
market production is much less than in total production. For example only
26% of marketed milk supply comes from private farms as against 89% of total
milk sunply. This illustrates the imnortance of social agriculture in the
generation of a marketed surplus to fuel industrial growth.
7.        The main reason for the increasing share of the social sector in
:tgricultuiral production is that it absorbed more than two-thirds of the
gross fixed investment in agriculture in the post war period.  The relative



ANNEX 1
Page 4
neglect of the private sector meant that almost all the investment on the
peasant farms were financed from own savings. Despite heavy investment in
social sector agriculture, the share of agriculture in total investment has
declined since the early sixties, a fact which is being redressed in the
Agricultural Development Plan (Annex 2). This investment pattern is re-
flected in an increasing capital intensity in social sector agriculture,
with the fixed capital per worker increasing from 27,000 dinars (1966 prices)
in 1961 to over 96,000 dinars in 1970. At the same time the capital output
ratio has increased from an average of 2.86:1 during 1957-61 to 4.43:1 during
1968-70.
8.        Other reasons for the more rapid growth of the social sector in
agriculture were (i) differences in the "intellectual input" - highlighted
by a 54% increase of university trained workers in the social sector between
1964-70 while the total number of permanent workers decreased by 28% in the
same time; and (ii) differences in the production potential of both sectors -
caused by the relatively greater importance of the social sector in the fer-
tile plains in the developed regions, and in the fertile valleys of the
mountain areas, especially in Macedonia and Kosovo. The rapid growth
of land and labor productivity in the social sector has resulted in a grow-
ing gap between the two sectors. Average yields per hectare in crop produc-
tion are almost twice as high in the social sector, while milk yields per
cow are more than four times higher. The relative differences appear to
be independent of the quality of land in wheat and maize production. In
milk production, the disparity is mainly due to differences in the volume
and quality of fodder produced, but also to the quality of the breeding
stock.
The Social Sector
9.        The social sector includes (i) agro-industrial kombinats (inte-
grated factory farms); (ii) agricultural estates (both general agriculture
and livestock farms); and (iii) general agricultural cooperatives with their
own agricultural production as well as provision of services to individual
farmers.  All these organizations operate under the principles of social
ownership of the means of production and workers' self-management. During
recent years many kombinats have established cooperative units either by
assimilation of existing cooperatives or by formation of new units, in order
to link part of the production of individual farms to their processing
industry.
10.       A notable feature of the development in the social sector since
the mid-1960's is the decrease in the total number of holdings by about 30%,
and the number of cooperatives by almost 50%. The decrease in the number
of holdings is the result of a process of mergers among kombinats and the
integration of cooperatives into kombinats. For, at the same time the
volume of production increased by about 28%, land input increased by 10%
while the level of investment remained practically unchanged. There was,
however, a significant decline in the labor input amounting to about 30%.



ANNEX 1
Page 5
11.       The main constraint to further development of the social sector
agriculture is the lack of capital for investments in crop and livestock
production and processing facilities. The social sector with its modern
production techniques and relatively high yields given the available land
resources and the climatic conditions, requires large investments in irri-
gation, drainage and land development to further increase production. Ex-
panded grain and fodder production would allow an increase in livestock pro-
duction without unduly raising imports of feed grains which are being re-
stricted due to Yugoslavia's shortage of foreign exchange.  The Agricultural
Development Plan recognizes the bottlenecks and emphasizes the priority for
investments in these areas (Annex 2, paras 8-11).

Private Sector
12.       Despite the decline in agricultural population, increased frag-
mentation and subdivision of holdings was a basic feature of small holdings
now numbering about 2.6 million. The Agrarian law, which limits the maximum
size of private holdings to 10 hectares of farm land or 25 hectares of wood-
land, has been changed recently with respect to the mountainous regions.
The number of persons that may be employed on a wage basis by private farmers
is also legally restricted, to a maximum of generally two persons.  Though
renting and leasing within the private sector is not restricted by law, it
exists only in rare instances.
13.       The average yields on private farms, although still significantly
lower than those in social sector enterprises (Table 2), for some of the
principal crops have risen sharply. For example, the average wheat yield
rose from 14 metric quintal/hectare in 1960-61 to 25 in 1971, maize in-
creased from 19.5 to 28 metric quintallhectare, of sugar beet from 140 to
285 metric quintal/hectare and of sunflower from 13 to 17 metric quintal/
hectare. In livestock breeding, the results were more moderate. Milk
yield per cow increased from 1040 to 1056 litres a year and the average
live weight per cow from 600 to 730 kilograms during the decade.



ANNEX 1
Page 6
14.       In almost all respects, private agriculture in the underdeveloped
regions is much behind private agriculture in developed areas. Livestock
froduction and breeding play a relatively greater part than crop growing
in the private sector for natural reasons. Modern methods of production
have not reached these areas: for instance they only have 15-20% of the
tractors as compared to 30% of the total cultivated area in individual hold-
ings. There are considerable regional differences in yields, which may vary
up to 100% for cereals between the less favored areas of Kosovo and Montenegro
and the most favored areas of Vojvodina and Croatia. In the breeding sector,
there are also very great disparities: in Bosnia-Herzegovina and in Montenegro
the average weight of slaughtered cattle is about half the national average.
The yields of dairy cows is also very low in all these areas compared with
Slovenia and Vojvodina.
15.       The main constraints for further development of agriculture in
the private sector are (a) lack of capital, (b) lack of credit facilities,
for the purchase of better quality breeds and seeds, fertilizers and tractors
and machinery, and (c) lack of extension services. In the Agricultural De-
velopment Plan an attempt has been made to overcome these bottlenecks by
providing capital and credit to the private sector and by sunporting coop-
eration between the social and private sectors. Moreover, at the republic
level, a number of measures have been taken to improve the existing condi-
tion of the private sector. Extension service centers have been established
and special funds are being set up extending loans only to private sector
farmers, particularly for the improvement of livestock. However, while
these facilities exist in a few republics they are inadequate. The progress
of helping private agriculture through social cooperatives has tended to
slow down (see para. 16). Furthermore, such cooperation is restricted to
areas surrounding the social sector agricultural organizations. For example
there are only few social sector agricultural enterprises in Kosovo and,
consequently, cooperation between the private and social sector in agricul-
ture is relatively less developed.
Cooperation Between the Private and Social Sectors
16.       Cooperation between the social and private sectors takes the form
of trade in inputs and output, provision of services by the social sector
such as supply of fertilizers, harvesting, cultivation, marketing and
credit, 1/ and the contracting of joint production. About 30% of all small
holders cooperate with the social sector in one way or another while 15-20%
participate in contract cooperation. Contract cooperation, in particular,
is mainly concentrated in more developed regions and with relatively larger
peasant farmers. The very small farmers, particularly in the underdeveloped
mountainous regions are least affected by such cooperation. There has been
recently a trend towards reduced cooperation. The reasons are said to derive
from difficulties related to the instability of the market and increased pro-
duction costs. Since the adoption of the "Green Plan" increasing emphasis
1/   Credit discussed in Annex 7.



ANNEX 1
Page 7
has been given to the strengthening of relations with the private sector in
agriculture. Closer links would help to increase the prosperity of peasant
farmers, thus preventing depopulation, particularly of the mountain areas,
and to exploit the private sector's apparent advantage in livestock produc-
tion.
17.       One important inter-relation between the private sector agriculture,
and the social sector in general (as distinct from agriculture), exists in
the supply of labor. An increasing number of peasants while living on the
farm are engaged in part-time non-agricultural employment, and this is an
important source of income for peasant households.
18.       Research, education and provision of technical services are provided
by some 50 independent institutes for research, dealing with specialized agri-
cultural problems. In addition, there are facilities for agriculture and
veterinary science at various universities. The institutes are financed by
the republics and the communes, and by payments for contract work done for
enterprises. The universities are the training ground for managerial staff,
and other technical workers. In addition there are some 200 educational
centers where farm workers are trained.
Foreign Trade
19.       The trade balance in agricultural goods has been mostly positive
during the 1960's, though strongly fluctuating, denending on variations of
yields of grain and maize mainly. Following a substantial surplus in 1970
the agricultural trade balance turned negative in 1971, mainly due to rising
imports of cereals. Exceptionally favorable export market conditions in 1971
for beef led to a small surplus in 1972. Rapidly rising imports of concen-
trates and other feed stuffs resulted in a large deficit in 1973 (Table 3).
20.       Leading export items of the agricultural sector are meat and live
animals, fruits and vegetables, beverages and tobacco, accounting for about
80a  of total agricultural exports.  Agricultural imports consist largely of
products not produced in Yugoslavia, such as tropical and subtropical fruits,
coffee, tea, cocoa, rice and increasingly protein concentrates.
21.       Food Processin.  One additional stimulus to the growth in postwar
agriculture was the development of the domestic food processing industry which
before the war had been very poorly developed. Apart from its beneficial
effects on exports (in 1970 processed foodstuffs constituted close to 30%
of all agriculture based exports), the growth in food processing capacity
led to an expanded production of high value industrial crops and vegetables
at the expense of cereals. In 1960 the share of industrial crops and vege-
tables in total agricultural production were 3.6% and 7.7% respectively.
In 1970 these shares had grown to 5.2% and 11.0%.



ANNEX 1
Page 8
Marketing and Prices
22.       In Yugoslavia there are first the "organized" social market for
agricultural products with "authorized purchases and sales", and then the
"informal" private markets based on peasant market places. The value of
authorized purchases amounts to 40-50% of total agricultural product at
present; the rest is either consumed by the agricultural population or sold
on the local markets. There is large regional variation in the relative
importance of authorized market production. In the less developed regions
of Kosovo and Montenegro it is only 10-25% of the total but its share in
Slovenia is more than 55%. Likewise, in the case of industrial crops such
as tobacco, sugarbeet, sunflower, market production is more than 70% of
total production while in the case of vegetables it is only 10%.
23.       Three types of pricing arrangements are used in Yugoslavia accord-
ing to the technical difficulties in -the implementation of price policy on
various markets. These are:
(a) Guaranteed minimum prices - applied for all important agri-
cultural goods (cereals, meat, wool) for which the imple-
mentation of a price guarantee is technically possible;
(b) Minimum prices, without guarantee of the government -
prescribed mainly for those agricultural goods which need
processing such as milk, sugar beet and other industrial
crops; and
(c) Prices free from intervention, for goods which are marketed
mainly on peasant markets or are highly petishable, so that
effective price control is practically impossible.
24.       The effectiveness of the minimum price system has not yet been
seriously tested; so far the guaranteed minimum prices were adjusted only
with considerable delay in accordance with changes in prices of production
inputs. Nor were actual prices prevented from increasing above the tolerance
limit of 10% above the minimum prices. The effectiveness of the price system
should therefore not be overestimated. It can smooth short-run price fluc-
tuations, at best, and then only if sufficient money and storage capacity
are provided. It is not able, however, to guarantee "production cost"
prices, or other desired price levels, if these do not correspond to market
prices.
25.       The price relationship between livestock products (with the excep-
tion of milk) and grain - especially maize - are at present more favorable
than in most of those European countries with an efficient livestock industry.
This situation has already stimulated an increase in production of pigs and
broilers, such that the continuing investment climate may create capacities
which will exceed the possibilities of the domestic market.

APPRAISAL OF
AN AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YTTGOSLAVIA
Agricultural Policies and Priorities
Institutional Framework for Agricultural Policy
1.        At the federal level, coordination of agricultural policies is
achieved by the Federal Chamber of Economy, and the Federal Committee for
Agriculture. The Federal Chamber has specialist committees to achieve co-
ordination of research, finance, etc., and the Committee coordinates the
plans and programs and legislation of the Republics. Responsibility for agri-
cultural policy implementation lies with the Republic Secretariats for Agri-
culture. Due to decentralized decision making, all functions associated with
policy implementation, rest with autonomous self-governing organizations, and
the Secretariats only retain indirect control via fund disbursements.
2.        Production plans are implemented by kombinats and cooperatives or
in some cases by special enterprises. Even though they account for only about
30 percent of total farm land the kombinats are an effective medium for policy
implementation regarding production and investments, since they have a virtual
monopoly of technicians and management skills.
3.        Policy coordination is achieved bv the Agriculture Division of the
Chamber of Economy since all social enterprises, banks, etc., are its members.
The Republic Chamber examines all legislation to be placed before the Assemibly,
forecasts markets, provides economic services and scrutinizes plans of enter-
prises. At the local level, the commune is the link between the community
and enterprises, and seeks to reconcile the various interests of the enter-
prises and the community, such as pressing for funds for enterprise develop-
ment, determining and regulating retail margins on basic farm produce, etc.
The commune and the republic also have some influence on the avaiJability of
credit to enterprises.
4.        Difficulties of orderly coordination, lack of enterprise response
to overall guidelines, and the non-participation of the peasant farmers in
the decision making process, are the major shortcomings of the institutional
system.
The Agricultural Development Plan
5.        The disanpointing performance of the agricultural sector in the
second half of the 196n's with annual production growth dropping below 2 per-
cent, and the growing awareness of the need to utilize more efficiently the
resources in the private sector and to slow down migration into urban areas,
initiated discussions on an integrated policy apDroach for the agricultural



ANNEX 2
Page 2
sector. In July 1973, the Republics and Autonomous Provinces agreed on a
common agricultural policy for the period up to 1985. This agreement, called
the Interrepublican Agreement on the Long-Term Development Policy for Agri-
culture (Agricultural Development Plan 1/) reflects the growing concern among
political and economic authorities that the country's increasing needs for food
can only be met with the cooperation of the private farmers.
6.        The Agricultural Development Plan's basic objective is to raise
production to meet expected domestic needs and allow for some exports. The
three sections of the Agricultural Development Plan include:
(i) foundations of the long-term development policy for agriculture;
(ii) measures for the implementation of the development policy for
agriculture during 1973-1975; and
(iii)  agreement on investments and financing during 1973-1975, and
on incentive policies for the private sector.
7.        The Plan emphasizes as the basic principles of the long-term agri-
cultural policy:
(a) the further development of the social sector through
modernization of production techniques, expansion of
processing facilities, better utilization of available
resources and the adjustment of production to changes in
demand; and
(b) the development of private farms primarily through co-
operation with the social sector, to ensure further progress
in the socialization of agriculture and rural areas.
The Plan's long run growth objectives were revised in June 1974 and are now
3.5 percent per annum agricultural production growth for the period 1971-1985,
with the social sector expected to grow at 7.5 percent and the private sector
at 2 percent. Emphasis is given to livestock production which is projected to
increase at 5 percent per year as compared with 3 percent for plant production.
S.        Within the field of livestock production the Plan gives high priority
to cattle breeding in both the private and social sectors of primarily milk and
meat producing breeds. The production of beef meat is largely intended for
exports, and, accordingly, qualitv improvement is emphasized; milk and milk
products are expected to be consumed domestically. The Plan stresses the need
for technical and organizational improvements in the collection and processing
of milk, particularly of the private sector. Emphasis is also given to in-
creased sheen production in the mountainous and generally poor areas of the
country.
1/   The Agreement is frequently referred to as the "Green Plan" of Yugoslavia.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
9.        The production of wheat and rye is projected to develop in line with
domestic need, including the formation and maintenance of an adequate level
of reserves. Production, now at a level of about 5 million tons per year,
is foreseen to remain at this level for the rest of the decade and to decrease
to about 4 million tons by 1985. Production of feed grains, particularly
corn, is planned to increase to about 13 million tons per year by 1985, to
meet the growing needs of dairy and meat production. Concommitantly, a
significant expansion in storage and drying facilities will become necessary.
10.       The production of vegetables, fruits and grapes is planned to in-
crease in line with rising living standards at home and increasing export
possibilities. The Mediterranean climate zone, where winter and early spring
vegetables can be grown, should provide the bulk for exports.
11.       The Plan considers the development of efficient food processing in-
dustries essential for the stability of agricultural development and the im-
provement of product quality. With rising standards of living domestic demand
is expected to shift increasingly to higher quality and processed products.
12.       The dominant role in the future development of agriculture is
intended to rest with the social sector kombinats and co-operatives. They
are foreseen to take the lead in increasing production and enhancing modern-
ization and labor productivity; they will be encouraged to widen cooperation,
strengthen socialist relations with private sector farms and expand cultivable
areas, partly at the expense of the private sector.
13.       The operational part of the Plan, i.e. sections (ii) and (iii),
covers only the period up to 1975. However, it is likely to apply, possibly
in a somewhat modified form, to the period 1976-80. It focuses on three
areas:
(a) price policies;
(b) investment policies; and
(c) social-private sector cooperation.
14.       The present price system 1, will be maintained in principle.  How-
ever, the basis for price determination will be modified:
Guaranteed minimum prices for cereals will be estab-
lished by interrepublican agreement on the basis of
production costs in social sector holdings. Whenever
production costs rise by more than 5 percent, minimum
prices will be adjusted. Market prices are intended
to be kept within the range of minimum prices and the
"+ 15 percent" level through interventionist purchases
or sales. Minimum prices for livestock products will
be fixed in relation to the average actual price for
1/   See Annex 1, paragraph 23.



ANNEX 2
Page 4
maize. The ratios aimed at are for pigs 1:8.5: for
heifers 1:10; for fattened sheep 1:6.9; and for broilers
1:8.9.
Minimum prices, as in recent years, are likely to be of little importance.
At expected market conditions actual prices are likely to rise above rather
than drop below the minimum prices. In the past the Yugoslav price system
has failed to prevent rising prices as inadequate storage facilities and funds
did not allow the build-up of sufficient reserves. Investments in cold
storage and grain drying facilities under the project should contribute to
make the price system more effective.
15.       Annual investments in agriculture until 1975 are projected to reach
4.2 billion dinars at 1972 prices. Annual investment targets for the period
1976 to 1985 were not yet agreed on in November 1974. It was indicated, how-
ever, that for the period 1976 to 1985 annual investments should be maintained
in real terms at the 1975 target level, i.e., at about 6.3 billion dinars at
1974 prices. More than half of this amount will be invested in the private
sector. About 40 percent of the financing will come from enterprises' and
farmers' own contributions. Commercial banks and state and federal funds
are expected to provide another 40 percent and the remainder is expected to
come from bilateral foreign credits, IBRD credits and special subsidies.
16.       For the social sector the Plan specifies the following sources of
finance for additional investments of 750 million dinars (at 1973 prices) per
year during 1973 to 1975:
1973     1974     1975
-- Dinar mill 
Basic Turnover Tax                         300      330       350
Counterpart Funds of Foreign Credits       165      165        -
IBRD Credits                                -       165       330
Other Domestic Resources                    -        90        70
The turnover tax funds are distributed to kombinats and cooperatives as
price subsidies for wheat (.10 Din per kg) sugar beets (.03 Din per kg),
and milk (.25 Din per kg); they are earmarked for investments and not subject
to redistribution to the workers. The counter-part funds of foreign credits
are distributed among the Republics and Provinces according to their share
in the production of the social sector agriculture. The Republics and
Provinces will channel these funds through their banks to social sector
enterprises and specify the investments to be financed with them.
17.       To mobilize enterprises' own resoturces for investments in agriculture
the Plan lists the following incentives:
(i) income tax exemption for investments in agriculture:
(ii) turnover tax exemption for investment credits to agriculture;
(iii) interest rate subsidies for investment credits to agriculture;



ANNEX 2
Page 5
(iv) reduction of import duties on agro-industry equipment not
produced in Yugoslavia;
(v) elimination of the dinar deposit requirement 1/ with the
National Bank for foreign credits channelled into agri-
cultural investments;
(vi) increase in the foreign exchange retention quota from
depreciation charges. 2/
18.       Investments in the private sector are projected to amount to about
3.3 billion dinars per year (at 1974 prices) in the 1976-1985 decade. About
a quarter of the required financing is 'expected to come from Republic and
Provincial budgets. The remainder will have to be provided by private farmers
and social sector enterprises that invest in the expansion of contract co-
operation with private farmers. Commercial banks will provide funds through
kombinats or cooperatives, as it will be the case under the project. There
may also be some direct lending to private farmers.
19.       Expanded cooperation among private farmers and between private far-
mers and social sector organizations is emphasized as the major medium to
increase productivity in the private sector. Such cooperation would enhance
modernization of private farms and mobilize more resources for investments
in the private sector. The Republics and Provinces have agreed to support
cooperative arrangements through provision of financing, and interest rate and
tax policies. They also agreed to pass legislation that would allow co-
operating private farmers to join the health and social insurance and pension
scheme of the social sector organization.
Evaluation
20.       The Agricultural Development Plan, for the first time in Yugoslavia's
postwar development, provides a comprehensive guideline for the development of
agriculture, encompassing both the social and the private sector. In recog-
nizing the potential of the private sector and explicitly supporting its
development, the Plan is a big step forward towards better resource utiliza-
tion and more even income distribution although the impact of the multiplic-
ity of interest rates on the achievement of these objectives should be studied
carefully. However, in limiting support to private farmers that either form
a cooperative or cooperate in some way with the social sector, the Plan will
not reach a sizeable portion of the agricultural population, notably the
smaller farmers and those in the mountainous and infertile areas.
1/   Enterprises and banks borrowing abroad are required to deposit a certain
percentage of the foreign credit in dinars with the National Bank without
earning interest.
2/   Enterprises are entitled to retain in foreign exchange 10 percent of the
annual amortization charges.



ANNEX 2
Page 6
21.       The Plan's projected growth rate for the social sector seems to be
too optimistic, unless a major transfer of land from the private sector is
assumed. The Plan apparently fails to recognize that the declining growth
rates in the social sector since 1965/66 were not accidental but rather
indicate a change in the growth pattern in that the social sector with the
land and technology available at present is approaching the level of devel-
opment where production increases become more difficult. Without an increase
in the land available to the social sector crop and fodder production is
limited to increase in yields. With the high level of yields presently
achieved the annual increase over a period of 12 years is unlikely to exceed
3%. Growth rates at or above the projected levels seem to be possible in
production lines where large production increases can be achieved with no
or little input of land, such as livestock production. However, their produc-
tion increase above 3%-4% per year would require increasing imports of corn,
which the Plan does not foresee.
22.       The planned growth rates for the private sector seem achievable.
They would, however, imply a growing income discrepancy between the private
and social sectors unless the labor force in the private sector would decrease
by about 5% per year. Migration is likely to continue, although at a lower
pace than 5% per year, leading to either an increase in the average farm size
or transfer of land to the social sector. An increase in average farm size
would lead to better resource utilization and increased productivity in the
private sector and would enhance contract cooperation which is concentrated
on the larger farms for reasons of economies of scale. However this is
limited by the present regulations on farm size. The latter alternative,
transfer of land to the social sector would be feasible only in areas where
the social sector is sufficiently represented.
23.       Apart from the widely practiced contract cooperation, the Plan puts
great emphasis on "various forms of cooperation" that will permit "large scale
production through highly developed forms of production cooperation on signifi-
cantly larger areas in the private sector". It seems doubtfull however, that
these forms of cooperation can be developed easily in the private sector.
The measures foreseen in the Plan tend to strengthen the economic position
of the larger private farms without offering persuasive incentives to join
in large-scale production cooperation.



ANNEX 3
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
AN AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Production and Marketing
Production
1.        Crop Production.  Yugoslavia's crop production over the last 10 years
has fluctuated significantly, as shown in Tables 1 and 2. Total production
of certain crops has shown annual increases of 70 percent, and others, decreases
of as much as 33 percent. 'Despite this great variability from year to year,
caused almost entirely by weather conditions, there is an overall upward trend
in output, most of which has come from increased yields. The area devoted to
crops also fluctuates significantly from year to year. Wheat area shows a
downward trend since 1969, even though total output has tended to remain un-
changed. The area under maize has also varied from year to year, but shows a
slight downward trend since 1967. This decrease in cereals has been accompanied
by an increase in industrial crops, notably, sunflower and other oil seeds.
2.        A major constraint on the development of cron production is the low
level of irrigation and drainage. At present, only about 100,000 ha or 1 per-
cent of the arable land has modern irrigation. In addition, there are numerous
traditional gravity irrigation systems, most of which are inefficient and
antiquated. The potential for irrigation in Yugoslavia is estimated at about
3.1 million ha.
3.        The need for irrigation varies by regions and depends on the amount
and pattern of annual precipitation. In the Danube, Sava and Drava River sys-
tems, irrigation provides supplemental water needed, especially, during the
months June, July, August and September. During this period, temperatures are
high and precipitation is erratic and often low. In the Danube-Tisa-Danube
complex the need for supplemental irrigation is even more pronounced. During
the vegetative period April - October about 600 mm of water is required. Over
a 20 year period, one-fourth of the years indicate a 50% shortage, and one-
fourth of the years a 33% shortage of needed water supply. Irrigation in these
areas would be supplemental to the normal precipitation pattern. It would
assure maximum crops each year and, thus, increase production by approximately
50%.
4.        The most critical need for irrigation exists in Macedonia and Kosovo.
In these areas, increase in crop production depends upon a full water supply.
In a large part of these Republics, without a full irrigation supply, the lands
cannot be utilized for any intensive farming. The ultimate program for agri-
cultural development will require storage facilities and perhaps inter-basin



ANNEX 3
Page 2
transfer of water supply. It is estimated that in these dry areas of
Yugoslavia where less than 50% of required water for crop production comes
from normal precipitation, a full water supply would increase crop production
from 60% to 200%. Without water, a large part of the area would remain in
natural vegetation.
5.        Water rights will not be a problem on the small scale developments
as proposed in this project. Water supply may become a limiting factor in the
drier areas of Macedonia and Kosovo. This factor places a limitation of model
development to sources of water supply which have adequate natural flows during
the vegetative period.
6.        The need for drainage, including both surface and subsurface drainage,
is most pronounced in Croatia, Slovenia and Montenegro where non-permeable sub-
soils prevent the full development of crop production potential. The total
area requiring drainage in the three Republics comprises about 1.2 million ha.
Drainage in some areas would enable full production from at present zero produc-
tion. Together with land development and consolidation it would be particularly
beneficial to the private sector. In many regions it would be a precondition
for efficient mechanization.
7.        The project's overall impact on crop production would be a reduced
fluctuation in annual grain harvests and an increase in the production of maize,
sugarbeets and forage crops. W4heat production would slightly decrease, by about
3,500 metric tons, as some wheat growing area would be transferred to vegetable
production. The project at full development would raise annual maize produc-
tion by 50,000 metric tons (less than 1 percent of the 1973 production),
sugarbeet production by 85,000 metric tons (about 3 percent of 1973 production)
and alfalfa and maize silage by 80,000 metric tons per year. The increased
production would be absorbed by the domestic market. It would provide for the
rising input needs of livestock production.
8.        Vegetable Production.  Vegetable production covering 3% of the arable
land is concentrated in Macedonia and Serbia Proper, where more than 80-85% of
total tomato and paprika production is concentrated. The private sector produces
most of the labor intensive fresh vegetables to be sold on local peasant markets.
The social sector concentrates on capital intensive production lines, such as
beans, peas and tomatoes, for industrial processing.
9.        Increased annual vegetable production from the project at full dev-
elopment would amount to about 115,000 metric tons of vegetables for industrial
processing, including mainly tomatoes, peas and green beans, and about 11,300
tons of fresh vegetables, including tomatoes, paprika, onions and fresh beans.
10.       Fruit Production.  Fruit production has been fluctuating heavily
with weather conditions (Table 4). The production of cherries, apples, pears
and peaches shows a clearly upward trend as the development of the number of
trees indicates (Table 5). The project would result in increased fruit produc-
tion by about 14,000 metric tons, including fruits such as cherries, straw-
berries, raspberries, blackberries and table and wine grapes. Incremental
production would vary between 1% and 5% of 1973 production.



ANNEX 3
Page 3
11.       Livestock Production.  The relatively more stable output trend for
livestock products is shown in Table 7. The fluctuations in output that have
occurred are the result of both weather conditions, affecting feed availability,
and adverse market conditions. Since 1965, beef production expanded at an
average annual growth rate of 2.5-2.7%, well below the consumption growth rate
of more than 4.5% per year. Fluctuations occurred during the 1965-1973 period.
Production stagnated between 1965 and 1970 at low cattle prices, varying in
the social sector between Din 5.5 and Din 7 per kg for fattened young cattle
and between Din 4 and Din 6 in the private sector (Table 7). Livestock pro-
duction picked up quickly with increasing prices after 1970. Annual growth
rates increased above 5%. However, market production grew only less than 3%
annually due to rebuilding cattle herds which declined under the influence of
prices and market conditions 1968/69.
12.       Production of beef and veal in 1973 amounted to '320,000 metric tons
of which almost 50,000 tons (carcass weight equivalents) served to increase
the cattle herd. About 74,000 tons were exported, most of this (80 percent)
to the EEC, principally Italy. The project includes a beef fattening component
which would result in an increase of baby beef production of 3,300 tons. In
addition, there would be an increase of about 1,400 tons of meat from cull cows.
13.       Pork production has remained at about the level of the early 1960's,
but was subject to short cycles related to market conditions. There has also
been a marked expansion in the outDut of poultry meat and eggs. Both pork and
poultry are being produced to an increasing extent in the social sector, which
by 1973, accounted for 55 and 45 percent of total production respectively. The
project would increase pig production by about 3,000 tons, equivalent 1% of
annual production in 1973.
14.       The size of the sheeD flock is beginning to fall, and the output of
both wool and mutton is declining accordingly. This seems to be associated
with a shift of demand to pork and poultry products at the prevailing prices.
The sheep flock is held almost exclusively on private farms (96 percent) and
is comprised generally of low Productivity multi-purpose local breeds. In the
mountainous areas sheep are kept under extremely extensive conditions. With
no winter fodder prepared they are brought down to the Mediterranean coast or
to the valleys, at times even as far as Vojvodina, despite state and community
laws that borbid wandering. Lambs are usually born between February and March
and frequently sold from winter pastures at low weight. A change in this
pattern would require relatively large investments to provide for increased
production, harvesting and storage of winter feed. The Project in financing
these investments would result in an increase of 55,000 sheep, eauivalent to
less than 1 percent of the present sheep flock.
15.       Milk production amounted to 3 billion kg in 1973, 89 percent of which
was produced on private sector farms. Production stagnated between 1966 and
1971 and began to rise modestly thereafter. Production and marketing organiza-
tion of milk are inefficient in Yugoslavia. Average milk production per cow
amounted to 1,235 kg in 1973 after it had passed the 1,200 kg level already in
1966. Only 25 percent of the total production was marketed on "authorized
markets". The amount which has been marketed directly by peasants is not
known, but it is not likely to exceed another 15-20 percent. (Table 13).



ANNEX 3
Page 4
16.       Efficiency in milk production differs widely between regions as well
as between the social and private sectors. In 1973 milk production per cow
was more than 2,200 kg in Vojvodina and less than 1,000 kg in Bosnia-Hercegovina
and Macedonia. In the same year milk production in the social sector averaged
4,000 kg per cow for the whole of Yugoslavia while in Slovenia it passed even
the 4,500 kg level. The social sector markets almost its total production.
It is the major source of supply for the large cities. The private sector sells
less than 20 percent of its production to processing plants. The low production
and marketing efficiency in the private sector has the following reasons:
(a) Lack of marketing outlets in many parts of the country,
especially in the mountains. Farmers have only the choice
to either consume the milk or sell it in form of cheese.
Consequently, incentives to modernize or expand milk produc-
tion hardly exist.
(b) Low qualities of the cows, varying from high yielding Simmentals
via various types of cross breeds to pure local breeds, mostly
Busha, with low productivity in milk and meat production.
(c) Low productivity of fodder production, particularly on poor
grasslands in the hilly and mountainous areas with an average
fodder production of less than 3,000 kg of green matter per ha.
17.       The project makes an integrated effort to improve these conditions:
(a) By providing milk collection stations that would improve
marketing possibilities of private farmers, and raise farm
gate prices, especially in less developed communities with
lacking marketing outlets:
(b)  By financing investments in private farms that would improve
the fodder and livestock quality and, thus, raise milk produc-
tion and provide the basis for an increase in the production
of beef.
The increase in milk production at full development would amount to about 90
million liters, equivalent to 37 of total production in 1973. It would raise
per capital milk consumption bv 4.3 liters or 6% above the 1973 level.
Marketing
18.       The increase in production resulting from the project would be
absorbed predominantly by the domestic market. Only the cattle fattening,
fruit and vegetable comnonents are expected to produce significantly for
exnorts.
19.       Crop Production Market.  Yugoslavia traditionally has been a net
importer of cereals and feeding stuff for animal production. Net imports



ANNEX 3
Page 5
during the 1960's averaged about 500,000 tons per year with wide annual fluc-
tuations, depending upon weather conditions. With increasing livestock produc-
tion net imports of cereals and animal feed have shown a rising trend and with
planned increases in livestock production of 5% per year they will continue to
grow. During 1971-73 they almost doubled to an average of 950,000 tons per
year. The increased output from the project would reduce these import needs
by about 5 percent. iforeover, in stabilizing production it would eliminate a
major erratic element in Yugoslavia's balance of payments.
20.       Vegetable Market.  Most of Yugoslavia's vegetable production is con-
sumed domestically. There are some exports of tomatoes and paprika amounting
to 3% and 9% of their total production, respectively, in 1973. The main buyer
of Yugoslavia's paprika exports is the Federal Republic of Germany which
absorbed 68 percent of the total (Table 16). Germany's paprika imports since
1967 increased by more than 30 percent and are likely to continue to rise,
although probably with slightly decreasing growth rates. Yugoslavia has
maintained successfully its competitive position in West European markets
against increasing competition, mainly from Bulgaria, Romania and Spain. It
can be expected that with their closeness to the main market and long exper-
ience in marketing in this field, Yugoslav exporters will continue to be com-
petitive. Tomatoes were largely exported to the German Democratic Republic
(65% of total exports) as shown in Table 17. In Eastern Europe Yugoslavia
meets heavy competition of COMECON members, notably Bulgaria and Romania whose
total 1972 exports about equalled Yugoslavia's entire production.. Because of
poor market data and the presence of state trading, Yugoslavia's prospects in
the Eastern European markets are uncertain and difficult to evaluate. Western
Europe which absorbed about 10% of Yugoslavia's tomato exports may become more
interesting in the future; import prospects there have improved somewhat since
1968, although short-term forecasts of import needs show only moderate increases.
21.       Domestic vegetable consumption, excluding beans, has been growing
since 1965 at 3.5 percent to 4 percent per year, with slightly increasing growth
rates after 1969. (Table 18). Per capita consumption, almost 80 kg is com-
paratively high relative to the country's per capital income level. Production
and consumption since 1965 developed more or less parallel, although price
development suggests that there might have been a slight tendency of production
to lag behind consumption in recent years (Table 19). The increase in con-
sumption is likely to continue except for potatoes and beans. Yugoslav
sources estimate the annual increase of the future demand slightly above 4%,
based on an income elasticity for demand of 0.64, thus projecting a per capita
consumption of 114 kg (excluding beans and potatoes) in 1985, as compared with
71 kg in 1972. FAO projections, based on an income elasticity of 0.5, arrive
at consumption growth rates of about 3%.
22.       Seasonal prices have fluctuated in wide ranges during 1972-74, (Table
19), indicating the uneven suDply distribution during the year. While there
is some tendency towards oversupply during the main season, the supply of
fresh vegetables is low or non-existent between November and May. In summary,
it can be concluded that there is only limited room for the expansion of con-
sumption during the main season at prices that cover production costs. There



ANNEX 3
Page 6
is, however, a large and growing need for vegetables outside the major season.
This off season demand will only be realized if the supply is adjusted to
avail of it. The project takes this situation into account by providing for
investments in plastic houses to extend the growing season, for investments
to enlarge the storage and cooling capacity, and by emphasizing investments
in vegetable production in the individual sector in Macedonia and along the
Mediterranean coast line.
23.       Fruit Market.  Fruit consumption in Yugoslavia follows more or less
the development of supply. Imports are unimportant; only about 1% of total
apple and pear production have been imported since 1971. Exports play a
significant role only for soft fruits, i.e., for raspberries, cherries, black-
berries and strawberries. EEC countries, especially Germany, are the major
importing countries. Exports to other countries of Western Europe and to
Eastern Europe increased recently and the export structure is well diversified.
(Tables 20 - 22). Market prospects for soft fruit and cherries are excellent,
both domestically and abroad. The chances to export peaches to the EEC have
hardly been exploited so far, and good prospects continue to exist. The mar-
ket for apples and pears is limited to domestic consumption.  There may be
some rather uncertain export possibilities to East European countries. The
production growth that would leave prices at the prevailing level ranges be-
tween 3% to 4% per year. The consumption of plums, notably in the form of
slivovic is decreasing with the result of substantial supplies. Market condi-
tions are not likely to improve neither domestically nor abroad and production
will have to be drastically reduced.
24.       The project provides financing for soft fruits, cherries and peaches
where production is likely to lag behind demand at prevailing prices. Some
apple and pear production may also be included in the project to prevent in-
creasing imports in the future.
25.       Wine Market.  Domestic consumption increased continuously since 1965
with an average annual growth rate of slightly more than 2%. The volume of
exports increased by more than 50% since 1967, and in 1973 exports accounted
for about 10% of total production and for 35% of production sold on authorized
markets. Exports are regionally diversified (Table 23). Slightly more than
40% went to EEC countries, most of it to the Federal Republic of Germany (78%)
and to the United Kingdom (19%). The COMECON absorbed 39% of total exports,
of which 44% were exported to the German Democratic Republic, 40% to the USSR
and 16% to Czechoslovakia. Although export prices have increased steadily
since 1968 (Table 23), export possibilities, particularly in Western Europe,
appear less favorable than they were in the past. With production increase
in the EEC accelerating and consumption growth levelling off, competition is
likely to intensify. Yugoslavia enjoys a good competitive position in Western
European markets and, given its widely diversified exports, should be able to
maintain it. In the domestic market, demand has been projected to rise at 5%
per year, based on an income elasticity of 0.7. Demand, thus, would continue
to outstrip supply and prices are likely to rise somewhat.



ANNEX 3
Page 7
26.       Beef and Veal Market.  Domestic consumption of beef and veal increased
rapidly during 1965-1969, at more than 6% annually, when beef prices were low.
With rising beef prices in 1970 growth leveled off and consumption has stagnated
since. The pattern of price and consumption developments indicates a high
price elasticity of demand for beef and veal in Yugoslavia. Compared with other
countries of similar income level and preference structure Yugoslavia's beef
and veal consumption remained at a low level (Table 11).
27.       Foreign markets absorbed a substantial portion of Yugoslavia's beef
production. Exports during 1965-1973 shared between 23% and 46% of total pro-
duction, depending largely on the market situation in the EEC, notably Italy
(Tables 9 and 10). With the world beef crisis, exports into the EEC decreased
by 35% during the first 8 months of 1974 and came virtually to a halt in mid-
1974 when the EEC imposed an import ban on beef. Subsequently, some beef was
exported to the USSR and the Middle East, but substantially less than the
previous exports to Italy. As a result, prices decreased by more than 10%,
stocks of meat grew up to the ceiling of storage capacities and the number of
fattened cattle increased.
28.       Future prospects of Yugoslavia's beef production depends on the dev-
elopment of exports to the EEC and other countries and of the domestic market.
The present beef market in the EEC is characterized by an increase in the
cattle herd and in beef production as a result of high beef prices in recent
years. Mloreover, per capita beef consumption decreased because of both high
prices and stagnating or decreasing real incomes. As a result there was an
excess supply in the EEC during the second half of 1974. In 1975 production
is likely to continue to grow, following the increase of cattle herds in 1974.
Demand may be pushed up once decreasing producer prices are reflected in lower
retail prices. On the other hand, continuing economic recession may compensate
the expected effect of prices on demand and result, in summary, in a stagnating
EEC demand for 1975. Thus, Yugoslavia's export prospects into the EEC for 1975
can hardly be expected to be more favorable than the 1974 situation. However,
with economic activity picking up in the long-run, the EEC demand for beef is
lilcely to grow. Future EEC beef production will certainly be affected by the
continuing surplus situation on the milk markets, which can be solved only by
a reduction of the number of cows. Since most of the European beef comes from
calves from dairy cows the necessary decrease in the number of dairy cows will
also reduce the meat producing potential of the EEC. In summary, one can
assume that the EEC's import needs for beef will vary between 5% and 10% of
total consumption under normal market conditions. However, due to the cyclical
nature of beef production oversupply tends to occur in two of every six years.
(Table 9).
29.       Yugoslavia's share in total EEC imports varied between 10% and 30%
during 1965 to 1973. Its share tended to increase when imports were low and
to decrease in years with high imports, thus indicating a stable and strong
competitive position. Yugoslavia is likely to maintain this position, even
if it would have to direct a higher percentage of its exports to other member
countries than Italy. One can therefore assume, that Yugoslavia will be able
to maintain a share of at least 10%/1-15% of total projected EEC imports. Taking



ANNEX 3
Page 8
into account the lower import needs of the Common Market, Yugoslavia would
have to reduce its exports to the EEC to about 50% of the average level of the
1968-1973 period, or about 50,000 tons per year in the period 1975-1980. There
may also be some export possibilities in Eastern Europe and oil producing
Middle Eastern countries. However, prospects there remain uncertain and are
difficult to estimate.
30.       The major share of beef production will have to be absorbed by the
domestic market. Income and price elasticities for beef and the beef price
in Yugoslavia will determine domestic demand. The income elasticity of beef
meat in Yugoslavia is estimated at 0.6-0.8. Given the existing substitution
possibilities the price elasticity for beef meat is estimated to be between
1.2 and 1.5. The beef price, at the end of 1974 certainly below its "'long-run
equilibriumt" level, will probably recover to a level of 10%-15% below 1973
prices, i.e., to Din 15-Din 15.5 per kg of high quality beef. Under these
assumptions, domestic demand in 1980 will be somewhat above 300,000 tons.
Adding to this the expected exports of 50,%00  tons per year, Yugoslavia's beef
production would have to increase at an averagye rate of 3% per year to satisfy
total projected demand. W7ith. production growth rates during 1965-73 of 2.5%
the stimaulation of beef production by thc project appears justified in the
long-run. However, the market sittuation in 1977, when the project would begin
to supply additional production, is uncertain.  If production continuses to
grow at 2.57 per year, production in 1977 would total about 317,00(1 tons.
Assunming a per capita real income gronth of 5%' per year, an income elasticity
of .8 and a price elasticity of 1.5. total demqand, including exports, would
just about equal production, thus lcavins no room For additional production
from the project.  In view of thie uincertainties of the world beef market
situation it is recommended that all appraisals of cattle fattening subproj-
ects include a detailed export market analysis at the time of appraisal, and
that these subprojects, irrespective of the general free limit arrangements,
be submitted to the Bank for approval.
31.      MTilk !Iarket.  MTilk and milk products will be consumed entirely on
the domestic market.  Per capita consumption of milk and milk products is low
in Yu,osJavia (Table 14), mainly because of inadequate sunply in most cities.
Per  '.pita consumption increased at less than 2% per year during 1965-1973.
Assumine an average income elasticitv for milk and milk products of 0.5 and
a popuiation growth of 0.9% per year demand will increase at 2.57-3.5% per
year. Mlilk production is not likely to meet demand in the future, if present
trends rontinue.  Imports of milk products are likel  to increase rapidly, as
during 1970-1973 (Table 15) when they more than dotubled.  The increase of milk
Production from the project would satisfy partlv the expected growing demand
and help reduce the growth of imports.
32.       Market for Sheep Produietion.  Domestic consumption of mutton and
wool increased modestly during 1965-1973 with average growth rates below 2%,
with a tendency to decrease during recent years. The tendency towards stag-
nating consumption is mainly due to the lack of supply at prices that would
prevent consumers to shift to consumption of pork or poultry meat. Exports



ANNEX 3
Page 9
decreased by 35% between 1967 and 1973, from 4,600 tons to less than 3,000
tons, although export prices increased drastically from Din 16 per kg carcass
weight in 1968 to Din 45 per kg in 1974. The share of exports in total pro-
duction decreased from almost 10% in 1967 to less than 6% in 1973. Future
domestic as well as export demand will continue to exceed production. The
project would help to reduce the gap.
33.       In summary, the project would result in a small production increase
of individual products in relation to the country's total production. In its
emphasis on products in short supply the market is generally assured, except
in the case of the cattle fattening component. The beef market, at this point
of time, appears uncertain and difficult to project. Provisions would be made
under the project to review the beef market prior to deciding on investments
in this field.
