ANNEX 7 (A)
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
AN AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Financial and Institutional Aspects
A. Credit to Private Farmers
1.        Prior to the early 1970's, credit to the private agricultural sec-
tor was small relative to the amount available to the social sector. Recently,
more attention has been focused on the private sector, and investment credit
is increasingly available mostly through cooperative arrangements with social
sector organizations. Independent cooperatives or cooperative units estab-
lished within combinats and marketing enterprises have been organized to co-
operate with private farmers.
2.        In order to receive short-term or long-term credit from a bank
through the cooperative, the private farmer enters into a cooperation ar-
rangement with the cooperative under which he will be provided with inputs,
technical assistance and mechanized services, and his production will be pur-
chased. This arrangement may cover the whole production or only part of it
and the farmer is free to enter into such arrangements with several coopera-
tives for different products. However, private farmers usually prefer to
deal with only one cooperative entering into contracts covering their whole
production.
3.        The contract usually consists of a declaration of intent for 3 to
5 years with a specific contract for one year. The prices of the goods and
services supplied to the farmer and of the output purchased from him are
stipulated in the contract. In case of significant changes in the market
prices, each party may ask for renegotiation of prices so that an adjustment
is made, albeit with some delay.   However, since the margin for the coopera-
tive is fixed, any increase of the sales price is passed on to the private
farmers. The accounts are settled at the time the farmer delivers his output
to the cooperative.
4.        For investment credit, private farmers are expected to contribute
part of the investment cost (usually 20%, but sometimes up to 60%) from their
own resources, either in cash, kind or labor. The cooperative aggregates in-
vestment proposals of several private farmers and submits them to the bank.
The cooperative does not charge any spread on loans for on-lending to private



ANNEX 7 (A), (B)
Page 2
farmers, 1/ since it is compensated for its costs by the benefits it obtains
through the cooperation arrangements with farmers.
5.        These mechanisms are quite flexible and may vary from Republic to
Republic. However the general description above fits most of the existing
pattern of cooperation between the two sectors. This system of cooperation
with the private sector appears to be efficient and adequate where social
sector enterprises are interested in cooperation. However, the cooperation
system is less efficient or inadequate in the poor and mountainous areas
where the social sector is poorly represented.
6.        Direct Credit.  Private farmers may also obtain investment credit
directly from a bank. Direct credit has been of little importance, partly
because the funds available have been small, and partly because the conditions
of the loan were severe (usually the-borrower has to make a matching deposit
amounting to 30% to 60% of the value of the loan; the deposit is blocked and
bears no interest for the duration of the loan). Direct loans are mainly
used for the purchase of tractors and mechanical equipment. They are most
frequent where the cooperative system is not well developed.
B. The Legal, Financial and Operational Framework
The Banking Law
7.        The basic Federal Law under which Yugoslav banks operate is the
Banking Law of December 1971. Major provisions of this law are the following:
(i) A bank cannot be founded by fewer than 25 founders;
(ii) A bank must have a minimum of Din 50 million as founders
capital. No founder can have more than 10% of the total
voting rights;
(iii) The founders of a bank have an unlimited liability in re-
spect of the bank;
(iv) The bank's statutes prescribe the powers of the various
managing organs, the minimum conditions of joining and
withdrawals of founders, distribution and voting rights,
etc.;
(v) The Founders' Assembly consisting of representatives of
the founders is the governing body of the bank;
1/   The Macedonia Agro-Industries Project (894-YU) is an exception:  the
borrowing rate of cooperatives is 6.75% and their lending rate 8.25%.



ANNEX 7 (B)
Page 3
(vi)  The Assembly appoints the General Manager, his deputy and
members of the Executive Board and Credit Committee;
(vii)  The Assembly approves the bank's annual Business Policy,
its interest rates policy, and distribution of income to
founders;
(viii)  Banks are required to make a yearly appropriation to a
"Reserve Fund" out of income, of at least 0.5% of the
total loans outstanding at the year-end;
(ix)  Banks are required to write-off bad debts first against
income. Write offs against "Reserve Fund" are only possi-
ble to the extent that income is insufficient;
(x)  A bank will be liquidated if it is unable to meet its ob-
ligations for more than 6 months;
(xi)  In case of liquidation the deposits of individuals with the
bank have priority over all other creditors of the bank.
Other Federal Directives
8.        In addition to the Banking Law, the banks have to comply with di-
rectives issued by the National Bank of Yugoslavia and Federal Government.
The main directives which have a direct implication on the banks' financial
operations are:
(i)  Liquidity Requirement.  The banks have to maintain a certain
proportion of their deposits with the National Bank of
Yugoslavia (currently 30% of sight deposits and 3% of time
and savings deposits).
(ii)  Foreign Exchange.  Only authorized banks can carry out for-
eign exchange transactions ultimately controlled by the Na-
tional Bank. 1/ The sources of foreign exchange available
to enterprises for import financing are as follows:
(a) Liberalized (or Free) List. For the import of
items under the liberalized list (60% of all im-
ports) foreign exchange is freely obtainable from
the National Bank or the foreign exchange market.
1/   All banks under the Project are so authorized, except KBSO, a member
bank of UBZ.



ANNEX 7 (B)
Page 4
(b) Retention quotas, based on a percentage of the
enterprise's export earnings (between 5 and 100%)
plus a proportion (between 10 and 40%) of the
enterprise's depreciation charge against fixed
assets.
(c) Special quotas, established by the Federal Govern-
ment for specific projects and for the less developed
regions.
(d) Suppliers' Credits and Foreign Borrowings. Although
enterprises can contract for a foreign exchange loan,
most of the foreign resources of the bank's clients
come in the form of suppliers' credits guaranteed
by a bank. Prior approval is required by the Na-
tional Bank and is based on the applicant's ability
to repay the suppliers' credits and/or foreign loans
from its retention quotas or special quotas. Col-
lateral dinar deposits with the National Bank are
required both against foreign suppliers' credits and
borrowings to be posted by the enterprise or bank ob-
taining the credit. Deposit requirements against sup-
pliers credits vary with the term of credit. On for-
eign loans there is generally a non-interest bearing
deposit requirement of 75% of the loan amount contracted.
(e) Foreign Exchange Market. For short-term needs, the
banks can buy foreign exchange on behalf of their
clients in the foreign exchange market which meets
twice a week in Belgrade.
(f) The bulk of the commercial bank's foreign exchange
transactions are mainly done on behalf of their
clients who bear the foreign exchange risk.
(iii) All enterprises in Yugoslavia including banks are required to
write-off, against income, a certain percentage (25% in 1973)
of total uncollected interest outstanding for more than 90
days at the end of the year.
Accounting System
9.        The Yugoslav accounting system is substantially different from the
system of western capitalistic countries. In preparing periodic balance
sheets, statements of income and expenditure, and other financial reports,
the banks do not adhere strictly to generally accepted accounting principles.
The major differences in the system and the difficulties, in reconciling
these differences are summarized below:



ANNEX 7 (B)
Page 5
(i) The branches, being autonomous units prepare their own balance
sheets and income statements. In consolidating statements
for the bank as a whole, it may happen that the individual bal-
ance sheets items of all branches are added up without eliminat-
ing inter-branch entries.
(ii) Banks account separately for different types of sources and
application of funds. Detail accounts are maintained for
each loan made by the bank differentiated by purposes and/or
source of funds. No routine record is kept of total exposure
to any one borrower, and because of the large number of ac-
counts involved, compilation of such information is difficult,
except by obtaining information directly from the borrower.
(iii) The banks, operating as commercial banks to their clients,
observe strict confidentiality as regards financial informa-
tion received from their clients.
(iv) Some banks, and especially those in the less developed regions
do not attempt generally to make a periodic assessment of po-
tential losses within their loan portfolios.
Audits and Taxation
10.       As for all enterprises in Yugoslavia, annual statements are certi-
fied by the Social Accounting Service. In the work done by the SAS, the main
emphasis is on ensuring that the banks' and enterprises' operations are
within the various legal requirements, that the statements are in agree-
ment with records kept by the banks, and generally to ensure correct charges
of interest, allocations of net income, etc. There is no verification of the
existence of the various assets and liabilities to the banks and no opinion
on whether they are fairly stated. In addition to the SAS, the banks are sub-
ject to inspections by the National Bank of Yugoslavia who ensures, from time
to time, that the banks comply with the regulations on foreign-exchange
transactions and special deposit requirements.
11.       Under the new taxation system effective January 1973, the two
previous taxes were abolished and taxes are now levied on the interest re-
ceipts of the banks. As a result, taxes paid by the banks have decreased
significantly from 8% to 15% of Gross Income in 1971-72 to less than 3%.
For the majority of loans, a tax equal to 13% of the inteteest payments made
by the borrower to the bank is levied. Loans for permanent working capital
above 5 years have no tax levied. The taxes are passed on to the borrower
who pays the tax to the bank for repayment to the state. With the inclusion
of the tax payment the interest rates should be increased by a corresponding
amount, but in almost all cases the bank has reduced the interest rate so
that the global payment by the borrower would be the same. The tax payments
by borrowers to the bank do not appear in the financial statements of the
bank. The rate at which loans for investments under the Green Plan and loans
financed by IBRD under this Project would be taxed will be 2% of interest



ANNEX 7 (B)
Page 6
payments. Similarly lenders to the banks have to pay taxes on their interest
receipts, so they charge the banks accordingly. The banks pay taxes (13% of
their interest payments) to all Yugoslav business banks and to the Postal
Savings Bank. There are also minor taxes to be paid to the commune (land
use taxes) and Republican or Provincial taxes (especially in the less develop-
ed regions).
12.       Organization Structure.  The organization may vary from bank to
bank but common features are:
(a) Founders. No socio-political organization (i.e. commune,
province, republic) can become a Founder. An enterprise
(or a non-economic but non-governmental organization) is a
Founder of a branch of a bank and hereupon is a Founder of
the bank proper.
(b) Founders' Assembly is the highest governing body of a bank.
Each founder has only one vote, regardless of the amount of
his contribution, however a combinat may be represented for
example, by several of its units (plants, farms, etc.), each
with a vote, each being a founder.
(c) Executive Board is the chief management body of a bank
which is responsible for executing the policies approved
by the Founders' Assembly and other functions as deter-
mined in the bank's statutes including approval of loans
above the limit which the Credit Committee is authorized
to approve.
(d) Credit Committee of a bank generally consists of 15 to
20 members elected from among the founders and, sometimes
among the full-time employees of the bank. The only func-
tion of the Credit Committee is loan approval.
(e) Internal Control Committee is a committee elected by the
Founders' Assembly. It ensures that the provisions of the
law and of the statutes of the Bank are correctly enforced,
that funds are not misused and that functions are adequately
performed.
(f) Workers' Council is elected by the bank's workers and among
other functions it decides on appropriations of income dis-
tributed to workers and negotiates with the General Director
and the Executive Board on the distribution of income. As
part "owners" of the bank, they also ratify certain long-
term financial obligations, such as IBRD loans. The dis-
tribution of the annual income of a bank, after deduction
of taxes and appropriation to the reserve funds (for provi-
sion against losses), is governed by an annual agreement
between the employees and the Assembly.



ANNEX 7 (B), (C)
Page 7
(g) Organization. Banks have several branches within their
regions and accept deposits from customers and founders.
Branches of each bank are substantially autonomous units
with their own Founders' Assembly, Credit Boards and Chief
Executive Officers. The statutes of the banks, however,
usually limit the authority of the branches as regards
maximum amounts of loans which they may approve and/or
the sectors of the economy they may invest in. Large proj-
ects and projects involving foreign exchange financing gen-
erally must be approved by the Head Office. It should be
noted that branch offices are fairly autonomous entities
with a great deal of responsibilities in conducting their
business within the guidelines set by the head office.
Branch offices can separate themselves from a bank and
merge with another bank, on the decision of their founders.
(h) Management and Staff. The-General Manager of a bank is
elected for four years by the Founders' Assembly on the
basis of recommendations of a selection committee. Changes
of top managers are infrequent and professional competence
is the main criterior for appointment and promotion. Most
staff are economists, accountants, engineers and lawqyers.
C. The Bank of Vojvodina
Institutional Aspects
13.       Background.  The Bank of Vojvodina (VB) was founded at the end of
1961 as the State Bank of the Autonomous Province of Vojvodina with assets
of 0.9 billion dinars. In 1968, it absorbed several smaller banks by merger.
It covers most of the Autonomous Province of Vojvodina and most economic or-
ganizations of the Province are among its founders.
14.       Founders.  As of February 15, 1974, there were 486 founders broken
down as follows:
Industrial enterprises                    143
Agricultural enterprises                  135
Other economic organizations              167
Non-economic organizations                 41
The biggest shareholder owns about 8% of the Founders' Fund, but has only 13
votes out of 486 (2.7%). Each branch has between 8 and 130 founders, but
only 2 branches out of 16 have more than 50 founders, while 11 have less
than 20.
15.       Organization.  The Executive Board has 32 members, the Credit Com-
mittee and the Workers' Council each have 16 members. VB has a Head Office
and 16 branches, of which two are outside of Vojvodina in Belgrade and Zagreb.
The total staff of the Head Office is about 215 people of which 85 with an



ANNEX 7 (C)
Page 8
university education. Charts 1 and 2 show VB's organization for the Head
Office and for a typical branch office.
16.       Operating Policies.  VB has a Business Policy and other "decisions"
made by the Founders' Assembly to guide its operations each year. The maxi-
mum financial outstanding engagement of any customer should not excaed 30%
of the total outstanding loans of any branch office nor of the whole bank;
actually no borrower has more than 10% of the outstanding portfolio. In
addition to that, when a borrower has an outstanding debt to VB in excess
of 60 million dinars, the granting of new credits it may ask becomes the
responsibility of the Executive Board of VB. There is no centralized informa-
tion system to know in VB what is the outstanding debt of a given enterprise,
although the enterprise, when asking for a loan, has to indicate all its
liabilities. Since credits are basically granted by founders, an even distri-
bution of loans among enterprises may be expected, although the risk of exces-
sive demand of bank funds remains. The limit of indebtedness of the bank is
also fixed by the Founders' Assembly. In 1973 it was 5.3 billion dinars in
local currency and 0.2 billion dinars in foreign exchange and in 1974 it
became 7.6 billion in local currency and 1 billion in foreign exchange.
Appraisal Procedures
17.       VB discusses the proposed projects informally with its prospective
borrowers before they submit their credit applications. The credit applica-
tion of the borrower consists of an investment program usually in three
parts: technical, marketing and financial data. Feasibility studies to
prepare the investment program are sometimes retroactively financed by VB.
The credit application will usually go to a branch office and be studied there
unless its amount is above the free limit of the branch office (varying bet-
ween 1 and 15 million dinars depending on the branch office; branch offices
have also a limit on short term credits and a global upper limit). If the
credit is going to be in foreign exchange or if for any reason (e.g. technical
competence) the branch office staff is not able to process the application,
it is forwarded to the Head Office. In the Head Office, appraisal reports
are prepared by the responsible departments with the help of the advisers in
case of very complex projects. The Credit Committee of the Head Office decides
on projects below 30 million dinars and the Executive Board is responsible
above that limit. Projects submitted to the Credit Committee or the Executive
Board are almost always approved subject however to changes in the terms
and conditions of loans.
18.       Several weaknesses are apparent in VB appraisal reports:
(i) As far as technical data is concerned the appraisal reports
present the project as indicated in the credit application.
Normally no critical technical appraisal is made and the data
provided by the borrower is not verified. VB has neither the
staff nor the desire to check the borrower's assumptions. Only



ANNEX 7 (C)
Page 9
in exceptional cases, in the case of unfamiliar enterprises is
a consultant hired to do some technical analysis. The effect
of employment is considered in the number of jobs created, but
no evaluation of labor versus capital intensive alternatives
is made. VB does not check if there is a possible overinvest-
ment. The production forecasts are not checked.
(ii) As far as marketing is concerned, no critical study is made
either. The data of the credit application, which must con-
tain some evidence of availability of raw materials and of
future sales is not checked. In the case of exportable pro-
duction, only an evidence of future purchase by the Yugoslav
export organization is needed, with no check by VB of real
export possibilities. In general it is asserted that there
are no marketing problems.
(iii)  The appraisal report is mainly concerned with the financial
analysis of the borrower, its credit worthiness and its
ability to repay. Creditworthiness is based on past finan-
cial standing. Only when the depreciation resources are not
sufficient to cover future repayments is a detailed study of
financial projections made, but normally no financial analysis
of the project, as usually understood is made.
19.       Approval and Supervision.  Loans are approved by the Credit Com-
mittee of the branch office if below their free limit, by the Credit Com-
r,ittee of the 1lead Office if above the branch limit (or in foreign exchange)
and below 30 million dinars, and by the Executive Board if above the limit
of the !ead Office Credit Comittee. In 1973, the Executive Board considered
20 projects- 10 loans, 7 guarantees (one in foreign exchange) and three write
offs. Once the loan is anproved a contract is signed by VB and the borrower
under some conditions of effectiveness, such as the availability of other
resources or the signing of a promissory note. Normally VB finances only 40%
of the total investment costs of a project, so 60% of the funds have to be
secured elsewhere. In case of cost overruns the borrower is responsible to
provide the additional funds. Supervision is directed systematically only to
projects under construction or to problem projects. Operating projects of
borrowers who repay their loans on schedule are not normally supervised. VB
being also a commercial bank (and therefore making short-term loans to its
clients) maintains close contact with its clients' operations and financial
position.
20.       Procurement and Disbursement.  VB ensures that its clients observe
the laws and regulations on procurement (advertising, bid opening, award,
etc.). It exchanges information on these matters with other agencies (SAS,
State Inspection Corps). Disbursements are made on contractors notes, bills
or other evidence of supply; large amounts are usually disbursed directly
to the suppliers.



ANNEX 7 (C)
Page 10
Operations
21.       VB operates as a mixed commercial/investment bank.  With the ex-
ception of negligible equity investments in other banks and in one indus-
trial company investment financing is through long-term loans. VB also has
some special financing programs (livestock development, Federal Food Admin-
istration) and manages at no risk and on a commission basis funds on behalf
of socio-political entities (former state capital). VB is authorized to
deal in foreign exchange operations.
22.       Short-term loan disbursements are substantially the same as com-
mitments and time lag between the two is negligible (Table 1). Between 28%
and 33% of the local currency short-term loans approved have gone to agricul-
ture. Between 25 and 30% of the short-term loans. (including all those in for-
eign exchange) are granted by the Head Office of VB. Short-term loan port-
folio as a percentage of total assets has varied around 30%. It is estimated
that about 90% of all short-term loans go to founders of VB. The National
Bank rediscounts a certain percentage (25 to 50%) of some short term loans.
There have been no guarantees for short term loans.
23.       Long-Term Loans and Guarantees (Tables 1 and 2).  Long-term loan
approvals are usually closely followed by commitments, although some condi-
tions of effectiveness have sometimes to be met. Long term foreign exchange
loans amount to 3% to 7% of total long term loans, but there were none in 1973.
The foreign exchange costs of projects are usually financed through suppliers'
credits or with the own foreign exchange resources of the enterprises. Sup-
pliers' credits are often guaranteed by VB. More than half of the long term
loans are approved by the Head Office. More than 60% go to founders of VB
and about 80% are for investments in Vojvodina.
24.       Long-term loan approvals to agriculture account for an important
portion of total long-term loans, infrastructure (power, transportation) and
industry being the other important sectors.

ANNEX 7 (C)
Page 12
Financial AsDects
25.       Short Term Resources.  As a general policy, the short-term re-
sources are used almost exclusively for short-term investments and long-term
resources for long-term investments. Although before the 1971 Banking Law,
short-term resources could not be used for long-term investments, since then,
the National Bank may allow prudent usage of short-term resources for long-
term investments, as is the case with VB. Most short-term resources are
deposits and borrowings from the National Bank through rediscount facilities.
There are about 100,000 deposits of which 10,000 in foreign exchange.
26.       Long-Term Resources.  The resource gap (excess of commitments over
available resources) appears low for VB (especially when compared with banks
in the less developed regions) since VB generally does not commit unsecured
resources, wqhile the banks in the less developed regions do so with respect
to the Federal Fund for Development. The absolute resource gap, however, is
important since many project applications are not approved by VB because of
resources constraints. Also VB provides guarantees for suppliers' credit,
but does not make provisions from its long-term resources against these
contingent liabilities. Guarantees amount to close to 50% of long term
loans but defaults have been minimal (about 2%) and the risk does not appear
significant.
27.       Profitability (Table 3).  VB like all Yugoslav banks, does not
consider profitability an essential measure of its efficiency and financial
performance. Certain key features can be noted:
(a) Gross income less direct financial expenses in 1973 was about
1.7% of average total assets, which is low and reflects both
the mixed nature and the non-profit orientation of VB.
(b) Administrative expenses in 1973 were about 0.6% of average
total assets, which is also low and shows strict control over
the administrative budget.
(c) "Dividends", i.e. cash payments to founders accounted for
2.6% of the Founders' Funds in 1971, 1.9% in 1972 and 4.5%
in 1973.
(d) On the other hand, allocation to the funds of the bank as a
working organization were high (17% to 23% of the funds) but
part of these allocations were in fact extra bonuses to em-
ployees of VB.
28.       Financial Position (Table 4).  Current ratios have been slightly
above 0.8:1 which is low, but not of concern since VB has to comply with
the liquidity requirements of the National Bank. The long-term debt/equity
ratio is high 4.6:1 in 1973, although not so high than in other Yugoslav
banks but it does not fully reflect the financial cushion available to



ANNEX 7 (C)
Page 13
creditors since a substantial part of the debt comes from socio-political
funds. If debt to socio-political units is excluded, the long-term debt/
equity ratio becomes 3.1:1 which is adequate.
29.       Portfolio.  Since VB's financial statements have not been audited
according to internationally accepted standards, an evaluation of VB's quality
of investment portfolio and adequacy of provisions is difficult. Detailed
accounting records are maintained for each loan, but no aggregation of data
is made by borrower. This is not a problem for VB when appraising an in-
vestment project or supervising a borrower's financial position since VB
has access to the borrower's financial statement and the list of its debts.
It is, however, a problem in attempting to evaluate VB's financial position.
30.       VB periodically  reviews its portfolio to determine requirements
for provisions against potential losses. Each year, whole portfolio is re-
viewed, paying particular attention to loans to enterprises under legal
proceedings. As a result, an estimate of the amount to be set aside from
the income into a fund for write off provisions is made. If bankruptcy
occurs, and it is clear the funds will not be recovered, the amount to be
written off is estimated and the loan is cancelled and the fund for write
off provisions is decreased accordingly. These proposals have to be en-
dorsed by the Founders' Assembly. The compulsory reserve fund is a legal
requirement and is a fund of last resort; it is not normally used for write
offs. For 1972, loans for the following global amounts were examined:

31.       In 1973, the total amounts of loans under investigation was 35 mil-
lion dinars (0.35% of total loans outstandings) and write offs amounted to
12 million dinars (guarantees made good amounted to 11.7 million dinars).
This proportion of loans under investigation is very low and seems to repre-
sent more the soundness of VB's portfolio than an insufficient coverage of
the investigation. A high proportion of foreign exchange loans are under
investigation.  Write offs were actually as follows (Table 6):

Legal requirements for allocation to the reserve funds are an annual provi-
sion of 0.5% of total loans outstanding until the reserve funds reach 5% of
outstanding loans. In 1973, they amounted to 148.9 million dinars or 1.5%
of loans outstanding (2.2% of long term loans), that is more than 12 times
the amounts written off.
32.       At year end 1973, 41 borrowers had loans in arrears (all in the
social sector) out of more than 1,000-borrowing enterprises. The total
arrears (62.2 million dinars) all were for less than one year and accounted
for only 0.6% of total loans outstanding (0.9% of investment portfolio).
The distribution was: less than 3 months, 55.3 million; 3 to 6 months,
5.3 million and 6 to 12 months 1.5 million. Reschedulings amounted in 1973
to 1.2 million dinars. It should be noted that due to inefficiencies in
VB's accounting system, data on portfolio affected by arrears is incomplete
and a judgment on VB's exposure to bad borrowers could be made only after
an audit has been performed.
33.        Creditworthiness of VB has to be evaluated in taking in account
the specificity of the Yugoslav banking system. Along conventional lines,
it is simply not possible, pending an audit, to establish the creditworthi-
ness of VB as an independent organization. In fact in the Yugoslav environ-
ment, IBRD's normal criteria of creditworthiness have a limited meaning.
However, for the reasons given below, VB should be in a position to meet
its obligations to IBRD.
(i) The Founders have unlimited liability toward VB's obligations.
Creditors of VB have legal recourse to all assets of Founders.
(ii)  VB  is closely integrated with the economy of Vojvodina and its
liquidation is inconceivable.
(iii)  VB has qualified and experienced management and staff.  Its
financial management has been adequate with no significant
losses, arrears have been low, and obligations have been met
satisfactorily. VB loan portfolio appears sound, and adequate
provisions for risk are made.
(iv)  The long-term debt/equity ratio of 4.6:1 (or 3.1:1 when ex-
cluding long-term debt to socio-political entities) is
satisfactory.



ANNEX 7 (C), (D)
Page 15
Forecasts
34.       At present, investments planning is limited to one year and it
is very difficult to obtain longer forecasts. Data presented here are based
on inadequate information and the margin of errors is probably higher than
usual. Forecasts are at best rough and based on inadequate information and
defective assumptions. Short-te-a loans are expected to increase by 13% to
20% per year, of which 35% to 40, will go to agriculture. Long-term loans
commitments will actually decrease in 1975, but will grow at about 12% per
year. Long-term loans to agriculture will still account for about 25% of
total long-term loans. Guarantees will decrease in 1974, but otherwise
remain at about the same amount of 600 to 650 million dinars, of which about
15% for agriculture (Table 7). On the basis of the estimated commitments
of short and long-term loans, a forecast of resources can be drawn (Table 8)
showing an increase of the over-commitment of VB over the next five years.
However, less and less long-term loans will have to be financed with short-
term resources. The forecast balance sheet (Table 9) indicates a yearly
growth rate of total assets declining from 17% in 1974 to 7% in 1978. The
relative importance of long-term assets declines below 50%. On the liabili-
ties side, the short-term borrowings decrease almost three times between 1974
and 1978. The current ratio increases to 1:1 and the long-term debt/equity
ratio increases to 6.5:1. It was not possible on the basis of available
data to project a complete income and expenses statements but the profits
forecasts are satisfactory (Table 9). Overall the financial position of
VB will remain satisfactory.
D. The Participating Banks
General
35.       Only one bank from each Republic and Autonomous Province will par-
ticipate under the project, namely:
Croatia: Udruzena Banka Zagreb (UBZ)
Serbia:   Beogradska Banka (BB)
Slovenia: Ljubljanska Banka (LB)
Bosnia-Herzegovina. Privedna Banka Sarajevo (PBS) 1/
Kosovo: Kosovska Banka Pristina (KBP) 1/
Macedonia: Stopanska Banka Skopje (SBS) 1/
Montenegro: Investiciona Banka Titograd (IBT) 1/
and VB will function as a participating bank (PB) for Vojvodina. UBZ
(Croatia) although a bank by legal definition can best be described as an
1/   These four banks, as recipients of the Industrial Line of Credit are
described in more detail in the Report: Appraisal of an Industrial
Credit Project, No. 364a-YU.



ANNEX 7 (D)
Page 16
institutionalized banking consortium of five member banks (Komunalna Banka
i Stedionica Osijek (KBSO)), Rijecka Banka (RB), Investiciono Komercijalna
Banka Split (IKBS), Kreditna Banka Zagreb (KBZ) and Privredna Banka u Zagrebu
(PBZ)).
36.       These eight banks in terms of total assets are among the first
eleven banks in the country, together with the Yugoslav Investmen- Bank,
the Yugoslav Bank for Foreign Trade (Jugobanka) and the Yugoslav Agricul-
tural Bank, which are all former federal banks, and their total assets ac-
count for 70% to 75% of the total assets of all the existing Yugoslav busi-
ness banks. Four of the PBs have a quasi monopoly in their Republic or
Province. Of the participating banks, only LB, and to a lesser extent BB
perform banking activities all over Yugoslavia.
Institutional Aspects
37.       The organization of the participating banks is conform to the
general description given in Part B of this Annex. Each of the five members
banks of UBZ maintains its own autonomy, but when the resources of one mem-
ber bank (financial or staff) are insufficient for a project, the member bank
may ask UBZ to intervene and all are jointly and severally liable for UBZ
operations. The staff of UBZ-central is very small (30 people) and its func-
tion is mostly one of policy and of coordination while the staff of the mem-
ber banks is doing the actual loan administration. The financial statements
of UBZ are not consolidated in the proper sense of the term and there seems
to be a great deal of double counting, since transactions between member
banks are not cancelled out. Each member bank accounts for about: KBSO -
5%, RB - 15%, IKBS - 17%, KBZ - 28%, and PBZ - 35% of the total assets of
UBZ. Each bank has branches in its own region and outside, between 7 branches
(IBT) and 150 (UBZ). Staff is large in all banks from 400 in IBT to 4600 in
BB. In each of the PB the unit responsible for handling long-term loans to
agriculture will be responsible for investment subloans under the project
(in the case of SBS, it will be the same department responsible for the
Macedonia Agro-industries Project); in each of these units there are several
agronomists and agricultural engineers, in addition to economists, financial
analysts and sometimes lawyers. In UBZ, one staff member of UBZ central
will coordinate the activities of the agricultural department in the five
member banks. Procedures of loan appraisal and administration in the par-
ticipating banks are quite similar to procedures in VB.
Operations
38.       All the PBs are mixed commercial/investment banks and all except
KBSO, a member of UBZ, are authorized to deal in foreign exchange operations.
Data on short-term loans are limited and not entirely reliable and are there-
fore not included here.
39.       Long-Term Loans and Guarantees.  Table 10 gives a summary of long-
term loans of each PB for 1972-73. Long-term foreign exchange loans amount

higher than commitments in the less developed regions, showing the shortage
of resources to meet demands. The share of commitments for agricultural in-

A measuire of the importance of these eight banks is that in 1972 they con-

1.2 billion out of 1.25 billion (96%).
40.       Table 10 indicates guarantees made by the banks, mostly in foreign
exchange, with relatively few of them being given for agriculture.
Financial Aspects
41.       Short-Term Resources.  Short-term resources are essentially used
for short-term investments, but prudent usage for long-term investments is
also allowed under the control of the National Bank. All the banks in the
developed regions and PBS were doing so at the end of 1973, sometimes to a
large extent as in the case of BB using 43% of its short-term resources for
long-term investments. Short-term loan portfolios as a percentage of total
a3sets vary between 16% (KBP) and 34% (LB).
42.       Long-Term Resources.  The resource gap (excess of commitments over
available resources) is fairly high in absolute terms and as a percentage of
total available long-term resources. Part of the long-term disbursements
come from short-term resources. In the less developed regions, the banks
commit the expected but unsecured resources of the Federal Fund for Devel-
opment; they can estimate quite accurately the amount they will receive.
There is no risk in doing so and overcommitments are only technical. The
use of short-term resources for long-term investments is high in the developed
regions but is not of concern since it is closely controlled by the National
Bank. The overall resource position of BB at the end of 1973 was negative
but it was only a temporary situation, which was promptly corrected. The
absolute resource gap is higher since many project applications are not
authorized by the banks because of lack of resources. Also the banks guar-
antee suppliers' and other credits, but do not make provisions from their
long-term resources against these contingent liabilities. Defaults however



ANNEX 7 (D)
Page 18
have been minimal and the risk does not appear significant. Investment port-
folio as a percentage of total assets vary between 50% (LB) and 73% (KBP).
LB appears as the most commercially oriented and KBP as the most investment
oriented of all the banks (Table 5).
43.       Profitability (Table 3).  Although the banks do not try to maximize
profits and do not consider profitability an essential measure of their effi-
ciency and financial performance, certain key features can be noted from their
income statement:
(a)  Gross income less direct financial expenses in 1973 was between
1.4% and 1.7% of the average total assets, which is low and re-
flects both their mixed nature and their non-profit orientation.
The spread was in general higher for the more commercially
oriented banks and lower for the more investment oriented
banks. The spread was higher in the previous years (1.8% to
2.2%) since taxes paid by the banks were higher (0.5% to 0.7%
of average total assets);
(b)  Administrative expenses have been between 0.3% (SBS) and
0.7% (UBZ, LB) of average total assets. This is low and
shows strict control over the administrative budget;
(c)  Net income has been 0.3% to 0.6% of average total assets;
(d)  Allocation to the Founders' Funds was between 1.5% (BB) and
4.5% (VB) of the Funds, it was either distributed as cash
dividends or used to increase the Founders' Funds.
44.       Financial Position (Table 4).  Current ratios are between 0.63:1
(BB) and 1.31:1 (IBT). They are of course lower for those banks that use
short-term resources for long-term investments (all have current ratio below
1:1). But since the banks comply with liquidity requirements of the National
Bank, low current ratios are not of concern. The IBT current ratio is too
high and shows use of long-term resources for short-term investments. The
long-term debt/equity ratios are between 3.4:1 (UBZ) and 11.5:1 (KBP and
IBT). They are higher in the less developed regions but a substantial part
of the debt comes from socio-political entities which are unlikely to force
liquidation in case of financial crisis. If this debt is excluded, the ratio
is between 1.3:1 (KBP) and 5.3:1 (IBT), which is adequate.
45.       Portfolio.  Financial statements are certified by the SAS but not
audited according to internationally accepted standards, as explained in para
10. It is therefore difficult to evaluate the quality of the investment port-
folio of the banks and the adequacy of their provisions. Only some of the
banks review periodically their portfolios to determine requirements for pro-
visions against potential losses. Others (including those in the less de-
veloped regions) make annual appropriations out of income solely on the
basis of legal requirements. Funds for provisions against losses accounted



ANNEX 7 (D), (E)
Page 19
for 1.7% (UBZ, BB) to 2.5% (IBT) of the investment portfolio at the end of
1973 and yearly allocation to these funds out of income accounted for 5.8%
(BB) to 13.2% (KBP) of Gross Income.
46.       Arrears account for 0.6~' (UBZ, IBT) to 1.7% (SBS) of the invest-
ment portfolio. They were covered in all cases by provisions against losses.
In any case data on portfolio affected by arrears are incomplete and a judg-
ment on the banks' exposure to bad borrowers cannot be made before a more
thorough review is possible as a result of an audit. There is very little
rescheduling. Write offs and payments on guarantees were also negligible.
47.       Repayment Ability.  It is not intended - and probably not possible -
to establish the creditworthiness of the participating banks as independent
organizations. However, for the reasons given below, it is likely that they
will be able to meet their obligations to VB.
(a) The banks and the governments of their Republics and Provinces
have entered into an agreement under the Green Plan to imple-
ment this project and it is unlikely that the banks would be
allowed to default on their loans from VB without strong cor-
rective action by the Republican and Provincial Governments.
(b) The Founders have unlimited liability toward the banks' ob-
ligations. Creditors of the banks have legal recourse to
all assets of Founders.
(c)  The banks have qualified and experienced management and staff
although some of the banks in the less developed regions (KBP
and IBT) appear less solid in this respect than the others.
Their financial management has been adequate with no signif-
icant losses, arrears have been manageable, obligations have
been met satisfactorily and long-term debt/equity ratios, when
excluding debt from socio-political entities, are adequate.
(d) The banks are closely integrated with the economy of their
region and their liquidation is inconceivable.
E. Availability of Dinar Funds
48.       Since the IBRD loan of 850 million dinars would cover 40% of the
project investment costs, PBs and subborrowers would provide the balance,
728.6 million and 546.4 million dinars respectively.
49.       Investors' Contributions.  Subborrowers, in addition to their owqn
resources, would have access to premiums provided from Federal, Republic and
Provincial budgets for priority investments in agriculture (para 16, Annex II).
It is expected that for the years 1975-80, resources available from these funds



ANNEX 7 (E)
Page 20
to the social sector would be about 2.2 billion dinars and about 890 million
dinars to the private sector. These amounts would be sufficient to cover
investors' contributions under the project.
50.       Counterpart Funds.  The counterpart funds would be provided by the
PBs from their own resources supplemented by special savings schemes, and
state funds.
51.       PBs resources for investments are provided from long-term deposits,
borrowing, in some cases short-term funds within the liquidity requirements
of the National Bank and Special saving schemes. In some Republics and Prov-
inces, a special investment fund for long-term loans to private farmers at
subsidized lending rates has been established. 1/ For loans to the social
sector, commercial interest rates (10 to 12%) are usually applied, but in some
Republics and Provinces, the rates are sometimes subsidized by state budgets
for priority agriculture investments and other governments have plans to do
likewise. Banks are expected to provide about 2.5 billion dinars per year
during 1975-80 for investments in agriculture.
52.       State funds may come from three different sources:
(i)  the former state capital funds, i.e. repayments of loans made
formerly from the State capital and now transferred to the
banks as managed funds, can be relent by the banks on a re-
volving basis. The cost of these funds to the banks is nil.
The funds going to agriculture will be about 650 million
Dinars per year for 1975-80;
(ii)  funds from the Republican or Provincial budget are usually
earmarked for a given commodity (livestock, vineyards,
tobacco) or for underdeveloped regions within the Republic
or Province. These funds are lent to the banks at between
3% and 5%. They will amount to about 800 million Dinars per
year for 1975-80;
(iii)  in the less developed regions, the Federal Fund for Development
is the main source of investment funds; it is lent to the banks
at 4%, except in Kosovo (3%). The part of the Federal Fund
allocated to agriculture will amount to about 320 million
Dinars per year for 1975-80.
1/   Lending rates to private farmers:
Croatia              6%         Bosnia-Herzegovina         4%
Serbia           4.5%-5%        Kosovo                   3%-4%
Slovenia             3%         Macedonia              4.5%-6%
Vojvodina            6%         Montenegro              4%-5%



ANNEX 7 (E), (F)
Page 21
53.       These funds will be sufficient to provide counterpart funds under
the project. The cost of these funds to the banks bears little relation to
the cost of capital, and subsidies from state budgets may vary during the
next few years.
F. Organizational and Staff Reinforcement Program
54.       In view of the lack of familiarity of the staff of VB and the PBs
with investment appraisal techniques used for IBRD credit projects and manage-
ment of a nationwide agricultural credit system, it would be necessary for
VB to reinforce its staff by undertaking a comprehensive organizational and
staff reinforcement program whose functions will be to:
(a)  assist VB's management and staff on subproject appraisal,
according to appropriate standards, project supervision and
monitoring, ex post economic evaluation and other matters
pertaining to the Project. Particular attention should be
given to the methodological aspects of subproject appraisal
such as financial, market and economic analysis, cash flow
and rate of return calculation;
(b) work closely with the management and staff of the PBs in
respect of point (a) above and assist in the coordination
process between VB and PBs; including assistance in establish-
ing VB's procedures for reviewing subprojects approved by
PBs, PB reimbursement procedures and assistance in procurement
procedures;
(c) train the staff of VB and of the PBs in appraisal methods,
including continuous on-the-job training, and, as deemed
necessary, prepare training material and instruction manuals
for these purposes.
55.       Experts and staff needed for this program would be economists and
credit specialists with expertise suitable to perform the above tasks; at
least one of the persons to be recruited should have had experience with
agricultural credit operations involving a national program in a developing
country. To the extent possible the staff in charge of carrying out these
functions shall be part of the Project Operations Department.
56.       VB would propose to the Bank for its acceptance, a final program
outline with details on the number, type, timing, and period of employment of
persons to be recruited. Once the program is agreed upon, VB would keep the
Bank informed on the progress and specifics of implementation including the
actual qualifications of the persons recruited, and the Bank would assist in
the training of VB staff, who, in turn, would be responsible for training
the staff of the PBs.



ANNEX 7 (G)
Page 22
G. Interest Rates Study
57.       Since commercial banks are not independent profit maximizing in-
stitutions, the credit and interest policy is controlled by the founders,
mainly borrowing enterprises, which tends to bias interest-rate policy down-
wards. Because of the inflationary situation, this implies a negative or
low real interest rate structure, which has several distortive influences:
(i) it fuels excessive investment demand, making control of
inflation more difficult;
(ii)  it encourages waste of capital in all its uses;
(iii)  it adversely affects the quantity and quality of financial
savings and investment;
(iv)  it creates scope for nonprice rationing of credit and for
arbitrary external intervention in credit decision.
58.       Also the non-uniformity of interest rates across the country and
the latitude for each Republic and Province and sometimes each commune to
subsidize interest rates for particular investments may run counter to the
general policy objectives of the couatry. For example in agriculture (which
is one of the sectors with nationwide planning) regional and local interest
schemes may be counter productive with respect to the Green Plan targets.
59.       Therefore a study will be undertaken to analyze the present inter-
est rate structure for the agricultural sector, and to determine whether
uniform interest rates would be effective and feasible. The study would
consist of three parts:
(a) an evaluation of the impact of the existing agricultural er
credit interest rates structure on:
(i) investment demand and inflation;
(ii) the choice of production techniques and capacity
utilization;
(iii) the quantity and quality of financial savings and
investment of agricultural social sector enterprises
and private farmers;
(iv) rationing of credit;
(v) the effect of non-uniform interest rates between
agricultural subsectors and investors, and the im-
plications for the realization of the objectives of
the "Green Plan", and



ANNEX 7 (G)
Page 23
(vi) the adequacy of provisions for covering the
administrative costs and risks of agricultural
credit.
(b) a review of other factors affecting the interest rate
structure, such as government policies, the banking structure
and viewpoints of agricultural social sector enterprises and
of private farmers.
(c) in the light of the findings of parts (a) and (b), a deter-
mination of whether a uniform interest rate system is both
desirable and feasible.
61.       In carrying out this study, relevant previous and on-going studies
should be fully taken into account. -VB would be responsible for carrying
out this study under terms of reference acceptable to the Bank and for com-
pleting it within one year of loan signing. It would consult with the Bank
on the staffing and timing of arrangements for the study.

APPRAISAL OF
AN AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Pro.jected Investments, Operating and Financial Results
The projected investment, income and cash flow statements for twenty-one
representative investment models are shown in detail in Appendices 1 to 21 of
this Annex.  The financial rates of return including those from sensitivity
tests are summarized in the following table. All prices, yields and other teen-
nical coefficients used in the models are based on information received at the
time of appraisal (November 1974). Although input and output prices are likely
to change over time, it is anticipated that they will increase at about the
same rate. Therefore, in all models, constant prices have been applied. A phy-
sical contingency of 5% has been included in the investment costs. The yields
anticipated are generally conservative, particularly for fruit and vegetable
production where risks are relatively high. Particular assumptions made for
each model are listed in the footnotes of the respective tables.
All cash flow statements have been computed on the basis of interest
rates on the subloans of 9.75% for the social sector subborrowers and of 7.75%
for the private subborrowers. In fact the rates on the portion of the subloans
financed out of the proceeds of the Bank loan would be ll/O for all subborrowers
and the rates on the portion of the subloans financed out of local ftnds would
be the prevailing rates on these funds, i.e. varying presently between 3% and
6% for the Drivate sector and 3% and 12% for the social sector. Considering
average lending rates of local funds across the country of 4.5% for the private
sector and 8.08% for the social sector, the cash flow statements as computed
reflect as near as possible the expected average interest rate to subborrowers.

ANNEX 10
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
AN AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Economic Rate of Return of the Project
1.        The economic rate of return was calculated by aggregating the rep-
resentative models as described in Annex 8 and adding training costs and those
for administering the Project. Benefits resulting from improved resource al-
location in the agricultural sector have not been included in the base case,
but their impact on the economic rate of return were tested in the sensitivity
analysis.
2.        The economic rate of return calculations are based on the following
assumptions:
(a)  Output Prices.  For wheat, maize, and beef the Bank's commodity
world price projections (in constant 1974 prices) were used as
basic data. The price of milk has been reduced by the subsidy
of Din 25 per kg; it has been projected to develop according
to the beef price. Prices of sugar-beets and sunflower se,eds
were reduced by the prevailing subsidies of Din .03 per kg
and Din. 20 per kg, respectively. The remaining products
have been priced at prevailing farmgate prices.
(b) Input Prices. Investment items have been priced at constant
end 1974 prices less import duties levied on imported compo-
nents. Prices of feed grains have been projected on the basis
of the development of the Bank's commodity world price esti-
mates. Prices of replacement cattle have been projected to
change according to the beef price. Other inputs have been
valued at end 1974 farmgate prices, adjusted by taxes where
applicable.
(c) Labor. Hired labor has been valued at the prevailing wage
rates. For investments family labor has been accounted for
at the wage rate for unskilled labor.  Family labor has not
been costed as operating expenses because there are unused
liabor capacities on private farms at the pre-project stage.
(d) Cold Storage and Grain Drying Benefits. Calculation of bene-
f:,ts from cold storage investments has been based on the price
d:fference of products sold at harvest and off season time and
reduction of losses. Grain drying has been assumed to result
in a reduction of loss and waste of maize.



ANNEX 10
Page 2
(e) Administrative and Training Costs. Costs have been based on
salaries of additional staff to be hired by the Bank of
Vojvodina and the Participating Banks.
(f) Exchange Rate. The exchange rate was devalued in October 1974
to Din 17 per US$1. It has been used for all calculations.
3.        Based on these assumptions, the project benefits and costs have
been calculated as shown in the attached table. The Project's economic
rate of return would be about 27%   The sensitivity analysis shows the
following results:
Investment Costs          +10%:   24%
Operating Costs           +10%:   21%
Benefits                  -10%:   18%
4.        Resource Allocation Benefits.  As the first agricultural credit
project covering all of Yugoslavia and involving eight banks the impact on
improved resource allocation will be significant particularly in the less
developed regions where misallocation of resources is compounded. The Proj-
ect provides for training of the banks' staff, that will handle, apart from
investments under the Project, most of the investment financing in the agri-
cultural sector. In the sensitivity analysis it has been - very conserva-
tively - assumed that 0.5% of agricultural investments, excluding those under
the Project, will be saved as a result of improved appraisal techniques.
This would raise the economic rate of return to 31%, thus underlining the
role that the Project would play in helping to improve resource allocation
in Yugoslavia.
5.        The economic rate of return indicates that the Project would re-
sult in substantial gains to the economy.
